</td></tr></tbody></table><p><b>Paul</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[a]</a></sup> (previously called <b>Saul of Tarsus</b>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[b]</a></sup> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 5</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 64/65</span> AD), commonly known as <b>Paul the Apostle</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997442_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997442-9">[7]</a></sup> and <b>Saint Paul</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Apostles_in_the_New_Testament" title="Apostles in the New Testament">Christian apostle</a> who spread the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">teachings</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">first-century world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009-11">[9]</a></sup> Generally regarded as one of the most important figures of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">Apostolic Age</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2001577Ch_32_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2001577Ch_32-12">[10]</a></sup> he founded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">several Christian communities in Asia Minor and Europe</a> from the mid-40s to the mid-50s AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoads199639_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoads199639-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> book <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, Paul was a <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> He participated in the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution</a> of early <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">disciples</a> of Jesus, possibly <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenised</a> diaspora Jews converted to Christianity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009345–346_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009345–346-15">[13]</a></sup> in the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem</a>, prior to <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">his conversion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-persecution_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-persecution-16">[note 1]</a></sup> Some time after having approved of the execution of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">Stephen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> Paul was traveling on the road to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Damascus" title="Early centers of Christianity">Damascus</a> so that he might find any Christians there and bring them "bound to Jerusalem" (ESV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> At midday, a light brighter than the sun shone around both him and those with him, causing all to fall to the ground, with the <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">risen Christ</a> verbally addressing Paul regarding his persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> Having been made blind,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> along with being commanded to enter the city, his sight was restored three days later by <a href="/wiki/Ananias_of_Damascus" title="Ananias of Damascus">Ananias of Damascus</a>. After these events, Paul was baptized, beginning immediately to proclaim that Jesus of Nazareth was the <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Jewish messiah</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> Approximately half of the content in the book of Acts details the life and works of Paul.
</p><p>Fourteen of the 27 books in the New Testament have traditionally been attributed to Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997407_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997407-23">[20]</a></sup> Seven of the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Pauline epistles</a> are undisputed by scholars as being <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">authentic</a>, with varying degrees of argument about the remainder. Pauline <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Authorship of the Epistle to the Hebrews">authorship of the Epistle to the Hebrews</a> is not asserted in the Epistle itself and was already doubted in the 2nd and 3rd centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[note 2]</a></sup> It was almost unquestioningly accepted from the 5th to the 16th centuries that Paul was the author of Hebrews,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownFitzmyerMurphy1990920,_col.2Ch_60:2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownFitzmyerMurphy1990920,_col.2Ch_60:2-26">[22]</a></sup> but that view is now almost universally rejected by scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownFitzmyerMurphy1990920,_col.2Ch_60:2_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownFitzmyerMurphy1990920,_col.2Ch_60:2-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKümmel1975392–94,_401–03_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKümmel1975392–94,_401–03-27">[23]</a></sup> The other six are believed by some scholars to have come from followers writing in his name, using material from Paul's surviving letters and letters written by him that no longer survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[note 3]</a></sup> Other scholars argue that the idea of a pseudonymous author for the disputed epistles raises many problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarsonMoo2009_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarsonMoo2009-30">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, Paul's epistles continue to be vital roots of the theology, worship and <a href="/wiki/Pastoral_care" title="Pastoral care">pastoral</a> life in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin</a> and Protestant traditions <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">of the West</a>, as well as the Eastern Catholic and Orthodox traditions <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">of the East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAageson20081_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAageson20081-31">[26]</a></sup> Paul's influence on Christian thought and practice has been characterized as being as "profound as it is pervasive", among that of many other apostles and <a href="/wiki/Christian_mission" title="Christian mission">missionaries</a> involved in the spread of the Christian faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009-11">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<p>Paul's Jewish name was "Saul" (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he" style="white-space:nowrap;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020748213">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">שָׁאוּל</span>‎</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew" title="Modern Hebrew">Modern</a>:</small> <i><span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">Sha'ûl</i></span></i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Tiberian_vocalization" title="Tiberian vocalization">Tiberian</a>:</small> <i><span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">Šā'ûl</i></span></i>), perhaps after the biblical <a href="/wiki/Saul" title="Saul">King Saul</a>, the first king of Israel and like Paul a member of the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Benjamin" title="Tribe of Benjamin">Tribe of Benjamin</a>; the Latin name Paul, meaning small, was not a result of his conversion as it is commonly believed but a second name for use in communicating with a Greco-Roman audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321-32">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, he was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">Roman citizen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup> As such, he bore the <a href="/wiki/Roman_naming_conventions" title="Roman naming conventions">Latin name</a> "Paul" – in Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Paulus</i></span> and in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">biblical Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Παῦλος</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Paulos</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strong_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strong-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup> It was typical for the Jews of that time to have two names: one Hebrew, the other Latin or Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisShort1879Paulus:_"a_Roman_surname_(not_a_praenomen;)"_38-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisShort1879Paulus:_"a_Roman_surname_(not_a_praenomen;)"-38'>[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECole1989_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECole1989-39">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jesus called him "Saul, Saul"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> in "the Hebrew tongue" in the Acts of the Apostles, when he had the vision which led to <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">his conversion</a> on the road to Damascus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> Later, in a vision to <a href="/wiki/Ananias_of_Damascus" title="Ananias of Damascus">Ananias of Damascus</a>, "the Lord" referred to him as "Saul, of Tarsus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> When Ananias came to restore his sight, he called him "Brother Saul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Acts 13:9, Saul is called "Paul" for the first time on the island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Cyprus" title="Early centers of Christianity">Cyprus</a> – much later than the time of his conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_Luke%E2%80%93Acts" title="Authorship of Luke–Acts">author of Luke–Acts</a> indicates that the names were interchangeable: "Saul, who also is called Paul." He refers to him as Paul through the remainder of Acts. This was apparently Paul's preference since he is called Paul in all other Bible books where he is mentioned, including <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">those that he authored</a>. Adopting his Roman name was typical of Paul's missionary style. His method was to put people at their ease and to approach them with his message in a language and style to which they could relate, as in 1 Corinthians 9:19–23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CathAns_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-CathAns-46">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Available_sources">Available sources</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historical_reliability_of_the_Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Historical reliability of the Acts of the Apostles">Historical reliability of the Acts of the Apostles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_(Michelangelo_Buonarroti).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg/220px-Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg/330px-Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg/440px-Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_%28Michelangelo_Buonarroti%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conversion_of_Saint_Paul_(Michelangelo_Buonarroti).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Conversion_of_Saul_(Michelangelo)" title="The Conversion of Saul (Michelangelo)">The Conversion of Saul</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> by <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, 1542–1545</div></div></div>
<p>The main source for information about Paul's life is the material found in <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">his epistles</a> and in the Acts of the Apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200319–20_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200319–20-47">[42]</a></sup> However, the epistles contain little information about Paul's pre-conversion past. The Acts of the Apostles recounts more information but leaves several parts of Paul's life out of its narrative, such as his probable but undocumented execution in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> The Acts of the Apostles also contradict Paul's epistles on multiple accounts, in particular concerning the frequency of Paul's visits to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">church in Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalemartin_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-dalemartin-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2000262–65_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2000262–65-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sources outside the New Testament that mention Paul include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_I" title="Pope Clement I">Clement of Rome</a>'s <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Clement" title="First Epistle of Clement">epistle to the Corinthians</a> (late 1st/early 2nd century);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a>'s epistles to <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Ignatius_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle of Ignatius to the Romans">the Romans</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Ignatius_to_the_Ephesians" title="Epistle of Ignatius to the Ephesians">the Ephesians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELadeuze1909_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELadeuze1909-51">[46]</a></sup> (early 2nd century);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Polycarp" title="Polycarp">Polycarp</a>'s epistle to <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Polycarp_to_the_Philippians" title="Epistle of Polycarp to the Philippians">the Philippians</a> (early 2nd century);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Historia Ecclesiae</a></i></span> (early 4th century);</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apocryphal_Acts" title="Apocryphal Acts">apocryphal Acts</a> narrating the life of Paul (<a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul_and_Thecla" title="Acts of Paul and Thecla">Acts of Paul and Thecla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Peter_and_Paul" title="Acts of Peter and Paul">Acts of Peter and Paul</a>), the apocryphal epistles attributed to him (the Latin <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Laodiceans" title="Epistle to the Laodiceans">Epistle to the Laodiceans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Third_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians" title="Third Epistle to the Corinthians">Third Epistle to the Corinthians</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Correspondence_of_Paul_and_Seneca" title="Correspondence of Paul and Seneca">Correspondence of Paul and Seneca</a>) and some <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic texts</a> attributed to him (<a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_of_Paul" title="Apocalypse of Paul">Apocalypse of Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Apocalypse_of_Paul" title="Coptic Apocalypse of Paul">Coptic Apocalypse of Paul</a>). These writings are all late (they are usually dated from the 2nd to the 4th century).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="764" data-file-width="1569" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png/280px-Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png/420px-Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png/560px-Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Broad_overview_of_geography_relevant_to_paul_of_tarsus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geography relevant to Paul's life, stretching from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Rome" title="Early centers of Christianity">Rome</a></div></div></div>
<p>The two main sources of information that give access to the earliest segments of Paul's career are the Acts of the Apostles and the autobiographical elements of Paul's letters to the early Christian communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200319–20_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200319–20-47">[42]</a></sup> Paul was likely born between the years of 5 BC and 5 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2007145–47_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2007145–47-52">[47]</a></sup> The Acts of the Apostles indicates that Paul was a Roman citizen by birth, but <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Koester" title="Helmut Koester">Helmut Koester</a> takes issue with the evidence presented by the text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoester2000107_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoester2000107-53">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was from a devout Jewish family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright1974404_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1974404-55">[50]</a></sup> based in the city of <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321-32">[27]</a></sup> One of the larger centers of trade on the Mediterranean coast and renowned for its university, Tarsus had been among the most influential cities in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> since the time of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, who died in 323 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright1974404_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1974404-55">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul referred to himself as being "of the stock of Israel, of the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Benjamin" title="Tribe of Benjamin">tribe of Benjamin</a>, a Hebrew of the Hebrews; as touching the law, a <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisee</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22-57">[52]</a></sup> The Bible reveals very little about Paul's family. Acts quotes Paul referring to his family by saying he was "a Pharisee, born of Pharisees".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200322_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200322-59">[54]</a></sup> Paul's nephew, his sister's son, is mentioned in Acts 23:16.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> In Romans 16:7, he states that his relatives, <a href="/wiki/Andronicus_of_Pannonia" title="Andronicus of Pannonia">Andronicus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Junia_(New_Testament_person)" title="Junia (New Testament person)">Junia</a>, were Christians before he was and were prominent among the Apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Romans|16:7_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Romans|16:7-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The family had a history of religious piety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disputed_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-disputed-63">[note 4]</a></sup> Apparently, the family lineage had been very attached to <a href="/wiki/Pharisees#Practices" title="Pharisees">Pharisaic traditions</a> and observances for generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup> Acts says that he was an artisan involved in the leather crafting or tent-making profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200341–42_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200341–42-66">[60]</a></sup> This was to become an initial connection with <a href="/wiki/Priscilla_and_Aquila" title="Priscilla and Aquila">Priscilla and Aquila</a>, with whom he would partner in tentmaking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup> and later become very important teammates as fellow missionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>While he was still fairly young, he was sent to Jerusalem to receive his education at the school of <a href="/wiki/Gamaliel" title="Gamaliel">Gamaliel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22-57">[52]</a></sup> one of the most noted teachers of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish law</a> in history. Although modern scholarship agrees that Paul was educated under the supervision of Gamaliel in Jerusalem,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22-57">[52]</a></sup> he was not preparing to become a scholar of Jewish law, and probably never had any contact with the <a href="/wiki/Hillel_the_Elder" title="Hillel the Elder">Hillelite</a> school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321–22-57">[52]</a></sup> Some of his family may have resided in Jerusalem since later the son of one of his sisters saved his life there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321-32">[27]</a></sup> Nothing more is known of his biography until he takes an active part in the martyrdom of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">Stephen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup> a Hellenised diaspora Jew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009242–44_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009242–44-72">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although it is known (from his biography and from Acts) that Paul could and did speak <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aramaic#Name" title="Aramaic">then known as "Hebrew"</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn200321_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn200321-32">[27]</a></sup> modern scholarship suggests that <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> was his first language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce200043_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce200043-73">[67]</a></sup> In his letters, Paul drew heavily on his knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic philosophy</a>, using Stoic terms and metaphors to assist his new Gentile converts in their understanding of the Gospel and to explain his Christology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee200613–26_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee200613–26-74">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKee1983208_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKee1983208-75">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persecutor_of_early_Christians">Persecutor of early Christians</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_(c.1600-1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8817" data-file-width="6711" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg/220px-Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg/330px-Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg/440px-Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_%28c.1600-1%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus-Caravaggio_(c.1600-1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Conversion_on_the_Way_to_Damascus" title="Conversion on the Way to Damascus">Conversion on the Way to Damascus</a></i> (1601), by <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a></div></div></div>
<p>Paul says that prior to <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">his conversion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[70]</a></sup> he persecuted early Christians "beyond measure", more specifically Hellenised diaspora Jewish members who had returned to the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009246–47,_277_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009246–47,_277-77">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-persecution_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-persecution-16">[note 1]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/James_Dunn_(theologian)" title="James Dunn (theologian)">James Dunn</a>, the Jerusalem community consisted of "Hebrews," Jews speaking both Aramaic and Greek, and "Hellenists," Jews speaking only Greek, possibly diaspora Jews who had resettled in Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009246–47_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009246–47-78">[72]</a></sup> Paul's initial persecution of Christians probably was directed against these Greek-speaking "Hellenists" due to their anti-Temple attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009277_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009277-79">[73]</a></sup> Within the early Jewish Christian community, this also set them apart from the "Hebrews" and their continuing participation in the Temple cult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009277_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009277-79">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion">Conversion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">Conversion of Paul the Apostle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%BAnior,_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco,_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3055" data-file-width="2508" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg/220px-J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg/330px-J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg/440px-J%C3%BAnior%2C_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco%2C_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%BAnior,_Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_-_A_Convers%C3%A3o_de_S%C3%A3o_Paulo_a_Caminho_de_Damasco,_Acervo_do_Museu_Paulista_da_USP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Conversion of Saint Paul on the Way to Damascus</i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1889</span>), by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ferraz_de_Almeida_J%C3%BAnior" title="José Ferraz de Almeida Júnior">José Ferraz de Almeida Júnior</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">Paul's conversion</a> can be dated to 31–36 AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromiley1979689_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromiley1979689-80">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett200221_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett200221-81">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992200_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiswonger1992200-82">[76]</a></sup> by his reference to it in one of his <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">letters</a>. In Galatians 1:16, Paul writes that God "was pleased to reveal his son to me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup> In 1 Corinthians 15:8, as he lists the order in which Jesus appeared to his disciples after his resurrection, Paul writes, "last of all, as to one untimely born, He appeared to me also."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the account in the Acts of the Apostles, it took place on the road to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, where he reported having experienced a <a href="/wiki/Vision_(spirituality)" title="Vision (spirituality)">vision</a> of the ascended Jesus. The account says that "He fell to the ground and heard a voice saying to him, 'Saul, Saul, why do you persecute me?' He asked, 'Who are you, Lord?' The reply came, 'I am Jesus, whom you are persecuting'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the account in Acts 9:1–22, he was blinded for three days and had to be led into Damascus by the hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup> During these three days, Saul took no food or water and spent his time in prayer to God. When <a href="/wiki/Ananias_of_Damascus" title="Ananias of Damascus">Ananias of Damascus</a> arrived, he laid his hands on him and said: "Brother Saul, the Lord, <i>[even]</i> Jesus, that appeared unto thee in the way as thou camest, hath sent me, that thou mightest receive thy sight, and be filled with the Holy Ghost."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> His sight was restored, he got up and was baptized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup> This story occurs only in Acts, not in the Pauline epistles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAslan2014184_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAslan2014184-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The author of the Acts of the Apostles may have learned of Paul's conversion from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age#Apostolic_Church_in_Jerusalem" title="Apostolic Age">church in Jerusalem</a>, or from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Antioch" title="Early centers of Christianity">church in Antioch</a>, or possibly from Paul himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcRay200766_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcRay200766-90">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Timo Eskola, early Christian theology and discourse was influenced by the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Merkabah_mysticism" title="Merkabah mysticism">Merkabah</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEskola2001_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEskola2001-91">[85]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Alan_F._Segal" title="Alan F. Segal">Alan Segal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Boyarin" title="Daniel Boyarin">Daniel Boyarin</a> regard Paul's accounts of his conversion experience and his ascent to the heavens (in <a href="/wiki/2_Corinthians_12" title="2 Corinthians 12">2 Corinthians 12</a>) as the earliest first-person accounts that are extant of a Merkabah mystic in Jewish or Christian literature. Conversely, Timothy Churchill has argued that Paul's Damascus road encounter does not fit the pattern of Merkabah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurchill2010_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurchill2010-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-conversion">Post-conversion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1434" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg/170px-Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg/255px-Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg/340px-Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rubens_apostel_paulus_grt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>St Paul</i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1611</span>) by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to Acts:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And immediately he proclaimed Jesus in the synagogues, saying, "He is the Son of God." And all who heard him were amazed and said, "Is not this the man who made havoc in Jerusalem of those who called upon this name? And has he not come here for this purpose, to bring them bound before the chief priests?" But Saul increased all the more in strength, and confounded the Jews who lived in Damascus by proving that Jesus was the Christ.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Acts 9:20–22<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_ministry">Early ministry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ananias_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ananias_house.jpg/220px-Ananias_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ananias_house.jpg/330px-Ananias_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ananias_house.jpg/440px-Ananias_house.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ananias_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The house believed to be of <a href="/wiki/Ananias_of_Damascus" title="Ananias of Damascus">Ananias of Damascus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1518" data-file-width="2034" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg/220px-Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg/330px-Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg/440px-Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Damascus-Bab_Kisan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bab_Kisan" title="Bab Kisan">Bab Kisan</a>, believed to be where Paul escaped from persecution in Damascus</div></div></div>
<p>After his conversion, Paul went to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Damascus" title="Early centers of Christianity">Damascus</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Acts_9" title="Acts 9">Acts 9</a> states he was healed of his blindness and <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptized</a> by Ananias of Damascus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHengel199743_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHengel199743-94">[88]</a></sup> Paul says that it was in Damascus that he barely escaped death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup> Paul also says that he then went first to Arabia, and then came back to Damascus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELake1911320–23_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELake1911320–23-97">[91]</a></sup> Paul's trip to Arabia is not mentioned anywhere else in the Bible, and some suppose he actually traveled to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a> for meditations in the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright1996683–92_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1996683–92-98">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHengel200247–66_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHengel200247–66-99">[93]</a></sup> He describes in <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Galatians</a> how three years after his conversion he went to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem</a>. There he met <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">James</a> and stayed with <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Simon Peter</a> for 15 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> Paul located Mount Sinai in Arabia in Galatians 4:24–25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul asserted that he received the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">Gospel</a> not from man, but directly by "the revelation of Jesus Christ".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> He claimed almost total independence from the Jerusalem community<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris2003517_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris2003517-103">[97]</a></sup> (possibly in the <a href="/wiki/Cenacle" title="Cenacle">Cenacle</a>), but agreed with it on the nature and content of the gospel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> He appeared eager to bring material support to Jerusalem from the various growing <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">Gentile</a> churches that he started. In his writings, Paul used the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_New_Testament#Examples_of_persecution_in_the_Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Persecution of Christians in the New Testament">persecutions</a> he endured to avow proximity and union with Jesus and as a validation of his teaching.
</p><p>Paul's narrative in Galatians states that 14 years after his conversion he went again to Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:1–10_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:1–10-105">[99]</a></sup> It is not known what happened during this time, but both Acts and Galatians provide some details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett2005200_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett2005200-106">[100]</a></sup> Though a view is held that Paul spent 14 years studying the scriptures and growing in the faith. At the end of this time, <a href="/wiki/Barnabas" title="Barnabas">Barnabas</a> went to find Paul and brought him to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Antioch" title="Early centers of Christianity">Antioch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009369_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009369-107">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup> The Christian community at Antioch had been established by Hellenised diaspora Jews living in Jerusalem, who played an important role in reaching a Gentile, Greek audience, notably at Antioch, which had a large Jewish community and significant numbers of Gentile "God-fearers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009297_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009297-109">[103]</a></sup> From Antioch the mission to the Gentiles started, which would fundamentally change the character of the early Christian movement, eventually turning it into a new, Gentile religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009-110">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>When a famine occurred in <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, around 45–46,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgg1962_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgg1962-111">[105]</a></sup> Paul and Barnabas journeyed to Jerusalem to deliver financial support from the Antioch community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett200583_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett200583-112">[106]</a></sup> According to Acts, Antioch had become an alternative center for Christians following the dispersion of the believers after the death of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">Stephen</a>. It was in Antioch that the followers of Jesus were first called "Christians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_missionary_journey">First missionary journey</span></h3>
<p>The author of Acts arranges Paul's travels into three separate journeys. The first journey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> for which Paul and Barnabas were commissioned by the Antioch community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2009370_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2009370-115">[109]</a></sup> and led initially by Barnabas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[note 5]</a></sup> took Barnabas and Paul from Antioch to Cyprus then into southern Asia Minor, and finally returning to Antioch. In Cyprus, Paul rebukes and blinds <a href="/wiki/Elymas" title="Elymas">Elymas</a> the magician<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup> who was criticizing their teachings.
</p><p>They sailed to <a href="/wiki/Perga" title="Perga">Perga</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylia</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Mark" title="John Mark">John Mark</a> left them and returned to Jerusalem. Paul and Barnabas went on to <a href="/wiki/Antioch_of_Pisidia" title="Antioch of Pisidia">Pisidian Antioch</a>. On <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Sabbath</a> they went to the synagogue. The leaders invited them to speak. Paul reviewed Israelite history from life in Egypt to King David. He introduced Jesus as a descendant of David brought to Israel by God. He said that his team came to town to bring the message of salvation. He recounted the story of Jesus' death and resurrection. He quoted from the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JewEnc:Saul_of_Tarsus_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-JewEnc:Saul_of_Tarsus-118">[111]</a></sup> to assert that Jesus was the promised Christos who brought them forgiveness for their sins. Both the Jews and the "<a href="/wiki/God-fearer" title="God-fearer">God-fearing</a>" Gentiles invited them to talk more next Sabbath. At that time almost the whole city gathered. This upset some influential Jews who spoke against them. Paul used the occasion to announce a change in his mission which from then on would be to the Gentiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1434" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png/220px-Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png/330px-Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png/440px-Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_missionary_journeys_stpaul_knecht.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the missionary journeys of St. Paul</div></div></div>
<p>Antioch served as a major Christian home base for Paul's early missionary activities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris2003_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris2003-4">[4]</a></sup> and he remained there for "a long time with the disciples"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup> at the conclusion of his first journey. The exact duration of Paul's stay in Antioch is unknown, with estimates ranging from nine months to as long as eight years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence-Jones201516_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence-Jones201516-121">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Raymond Brown's <i>An Introduction to the New Testament</i> (1997), a chronology of events in Paul's life is presented, illustrated from later 20th-century writings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_scholar" title="Biblical scholar">biblical scholars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997445_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997445-122">[115]</a></sup> The first missionary journey of Paul is assigned a "traditional" (and majority) dating of 46–49 AD, compared to a "revisionist" (and minority) dating of after 37 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997428–29_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997428–29-123">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Council_of_Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Circumcision_controversy_in_early_Christianity" title="Circumcision controversy in early Christianity">Circumcision controversy in early Christianity</a></div>
<p>A vital meeting between Paul and the Jerusalem church took place in the year 49 AD by "traditional" (and majority) dating, compared to a "revisionist" (and minority) dating of 47/51 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997428–29,_445_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997428–29,_445-124">[117]</a></sup> The meeting is described in Acts 15:2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[118]</a></sup> and usually seen as the same event mentioned by Paul in Galatians 2:1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> The key question raised was whether <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">Gentile</a> converts needed to be circumcised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup> At this meeting, Paul states in his letter to the Galatians, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_(brother_of_Jesus)" title="James (brother of Jesus)">James</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John</a> accepted Paul's mission to the Gentiles.
</p><p>The Jerusalem meetings are mentioned in Acts, and also in Paul's letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2007148–49_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2007148–49-129">[122]</a></sup> For example, the Jerusalem visit for famine relief<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup> apparently corresponds to the "first visit" (to Peter and James only).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Gal.|1:18–20_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Gal.|1:18–20-131">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2007148–49_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2007148–49-129">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/F._F._Bruce" title="F. F. Bruce">F. F. Bruce</a> suggested that the "fourteen years" could be from Paul's conversion rather than from his first visit to Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce2000151_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce2000151-132">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incident_at_Antioch">Incident at Antioch</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Incident_at_Antioch" title="Incident at Antioch">Incident at Antioch</a></div>
<p>Despite the agreement achieved at the Council of Jerusalem, Paul recounts how he later publicly confronted Peter in a dispute sometimes called the "<a href="/wiki/Incident_at_Antioch" title="Incident at Antioch">Incident at Antioch</a>", over Peter's reluctance to share a meal with Gentile Christians in Antioch because they did not strictly adhere to Jewish customs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910-127">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Writing later of the incident, Paul recounts, "I opposed [Peter] to his face, because he was clearly in the wrong", and says he told Peter, "You are a Jew, yet you <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">live like a Gentile and not like a Jew</a>. How is it, then, that you <a href="/wiki/Judaizers" title="Judaizers">force Gentiles to follow Jewish customs</a>?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:11–14_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:11–14-133">[126]</a></sup> Paul also mentions that even Barnabas, his traveling companion and fellow apostle until that time, sided with Peter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910-127">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final outcome of the incident remains uncertain. The <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i> suggests that Paul won the argument, because "Paul's account of the incident leaves no doubt that Peter saw the justice of the rebuke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBechtel1910-127">[120]</a></sup> However Paul himself never mentions a victory and <a href="/wiki/L._Michael_White" title="L. Michael White">L. Michael White</a>'s <i>From Jesus to Christianity</i> draws the opposite conclusion: "The blowup with Peter was a total failure of political bravado, and Paul soon left Antioch as <i>persona non grata</i>, never again to return".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2007170_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2007170-134">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary source account of the Incident at Antioch is Paul's <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">letter to the Galatians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:11–14_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Gal.|2:11–14-133">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_missionary_journey">Second missionary journey</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V%26A_-_Raphael,_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_(1515).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1182" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg/220px-V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg/330px-V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg/440px-V%26A_-_Raphael%2C_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_%281515%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:V%26A_-_Raphael,_St_Paul_Preaching_in_Athens_(1515).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saint Paul delivering the <i><a href="/wiki/Areopagus_sermon" title="Areopagus sermon">Areopagus sermon</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, 1515. This sermon addressed early issues in <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGrath2006_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGrath2006-135">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills20031109–10_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills20031109–10-136">[129]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Paul left for his second missionary journey from Jerusalem, in late Autumn 49 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKöstenbergerKellumQuarles2009400_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKöstenbergerKellumQuarles2009400-137">[130]</a></sup> after the meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</a> where the circumcision question was debated. On their trip around the Mediterranean Sea, Paul and his companion Barnabas stopped in Antioch where they had a sharp argument about taking <a href="/wiki/John_Mark" title="John Mark">John Mark</a> with them on their trips. The Acts of the Apostles said that John Mark had left them in a previous trip and gone home. Unable to resolve the dispute, Paul and Barnabas decided to separate; Barnabas took John Mark with him, while <a href="/wiki/Silas" title="Silas">Silas</a> joined Paul.
</p><p>Paul and Silas initially visited <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a> (Paul's birthplace), <a href="/wiki/Derbe" title="Derbe">Derbe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lystra" title="Lystra">Lystra</a>. In Lystra, they met <a href="/wiki/Saint_Timothy" title="Saint Timothy">Timothy</a>, a disciple who was spoken well of, and decided to take him with them. Paul and his companions, Silas and Timothy, had plans to journey to the southwest portion of Asia Minor to preach the gospel but during the night, Paul had a vision of a man of Macedonia standing and begging him to go to Macedonia to help them. After seeing the vision, Paul and his companions left for Macedonia to preach the gospel to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup> The Church kept growing, adding believers, and strengthening in faith daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Philippi" title="Philippi">Philippi</a>, Paul cast a spirit of divination out of a servant girl, whose masters were then unhappy about the loss of income her soothsaying provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup> They seized Paul and Silas and dragged them into the marketplace before the authorities and Paul and Silas were put in jail. After a miraculous earthquake, the gates of the prison fell apart and Paul and Silas could have escaped but remained; this event led to the conversion of the jailor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup> They continued traveling, going by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berea_(Bible)" title="Berea (Bible)">Berea</a> and then to Athens, where Paul preached to the Jews and God-fearing Greeks in the synagogue and to the Greek intellectuals in the <a href="/wiki/Areopagus" title="Areopagus">Areopagus</a>. Paul continued from Athens to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interval_in_Corinth">Interval in Corinth</span></h3>
<p>Around 50–52 AD, Paul spent 18 months in Corinth. The reference in Acts to Proconsul <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Junius_Gallio_Annaeanus" title="Lucius Junius Gallio Annaeanus">Gallio</a> helps ascertain this date (cf. <a href="/wiki/Delphi_Inscription" title="Delphi Inscription">Gallio Inscription</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> In Corinth, Paul met <a href="/wiki/Priscilla_and_Aquila" title="Priscilla and Aquila">Priscilla and Aquila</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup> who became faithful believers and helped Paul through his other missionary journeys. The couple followed Paul and his companions to <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, and stayed there to start one of the strongest and most faithful churches at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 52, departing from Corinth, Paul stopped at the nearby village of <a href="/wiki/Kechries" title="Kechries">Cenchreae</a> to have his hair cut off, because of a vow he had earlier taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup> It is possible this was to be a final haircut prior to fulfilling his vow to become a <a href="/wiki/Nazirite" title="Nazirite">Nazirite</a> for a defined period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDriscoll1911_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDriscoll1911-145">[138]</a></sup> With Priscilla and Aquila, the missionaries then sailed to Ephesus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup> and then Paul alone went on to <a href="/wiki/Caesarea_Maritima" title="Caesarea Maritima">Caesarea</a> to greet the Church there. He then traveled north to Antioch, where he stayed for some time (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ποιησας χρονον</span>, "perhaps about a year"), before leaving again on a third missionary journey.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some New Testament texts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[note 6]</a></sup> suggest that he also visited Jerusalem during this period for one of the Jewish feasts, possibly <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">Pentecost</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup> Textual critic <a href="/wiki/Henry_Alford" title="Henry Alford">Henry Alford</a> and others consider the reference to a Jerusalem visit to be genuine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PulCom_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-PulCom-149">[141]</a></sup> and it accords with Acts 21:29,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup> according to which Paul and <a href="/wiki/Trophimus" title="Trophimus">Trophimus the Ephesian</a> had previously been seen in Jerusalem.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_missionary_journey">Third missionary journey</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1186" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg/220px-Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg/330px-Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg/440px-Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eustache_Le_Sueur_-_The_Preaching_of_St_Paul_at_Ephesus_-_WGA12613.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Preaching of Saint Paul at <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Eustache_Le_Sueur" title="Eustache Le Sueur">Eustache Le Sueur</a> (1649)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrease2019309–10_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrease2019309–10-151">[143]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to Acts, Paul began his third missionary journey by traveling all around the region of <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a> to strengthen, teach and rebuke the believers. Paul then traveled to <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, an important <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">center of early Christianity</a>, and stayed there for almost three years, probably working there as a tentmaker,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup> as he had done when he stayed in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a>. He is claimed to have performed numerous <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a>, healing people and casting out demons, and he apparently organized missionary activity in other regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> Paul left Ephesus after an attack from a local silversmith resulted in a pro-<a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> riot involving most of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> During his stay in Ephesus, Paul wrote four letters to the church in Corinth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcRay2007185_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcRay2007185-153">[145]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Bible" title="Jerusalem Bible">Jerusalem Bible</a> suggests that the letter to the church in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippians" title="Philippians">Philippi</a> was also written from Ephesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul went through <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaea_(Roman_province)" title="Achaea (Roman province)">Achaea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bibleref2|Acts|20:1–2|NKJV_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-bibleref2|Acts|20:1–2|NKJV-155">[147]</a></sup> and stayed in Greece, probably Corinth, for three months<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bibleref2|Acts|20:1–2|NKJV_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-bibleref2|Acts|20:1–2|NKJV-155">[147]</a></sup> during 56–57 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> Commentators generally agree that Paul dictated his <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans#Dating" title="Epistle to the Romans">Epistle to the Romans</a> during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandayn.d.202_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandayn.d.202-156">[148]</a></sup> He then made ready to continue on to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Syria</a>, but he changed his plans and traveled back through Macedonia because of some Jews who had made a plot against him. In Romans 15:19,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> Paul wrote that he visited <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a>, but he may have meant what would now be called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illyria_Graeca" title="Illyria Graeca">Illyria Graeca</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurton200026_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurton200026-158">[150]</a></sup> which was at that time a division of the Roman province of Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetit1909_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetit1909-159">[151]</a></sup> On their way back to Jerusalem, Paul and his companions visited other cities such as <a href="/wiki/Philippi" title="Philippi">Philippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troad" title="Troad">Troas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>. Paul finished his trip with a stop in <a href="/wiki/Caesarea_Maritima" title="Caesarea Maritima">Caesarea</a>, where he and his companions stayed with <a href="/wiki/Philip_the_Evangelist" title="Philip the Evangelist">Philip the Evangelist</a> before finally arriving at Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Journey_from_Rome_to_Spain">Journey from Rome to Spain</span></h3>
<p>Among the writings of the early Christians, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_I" title="Pope Clement I">Pope Clement I</a> said that Paul was "Herald (of the Gospel of Christ) in the West", and that "he had gone to the extremity of the west".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a> indicated that Paul preached in Spain: "For after he had been in Rome, he returned to Spain, but whether he came thence again into these parts, we know not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Jerusalem" title="Cyril of Jerusalem">Cyril of Jerusalem</a> said that Paul, "fully preached the Gospel, and instructed even imperial Rome, and carried the earnestness of his preaching as far as Spain, undergoing conflicts innumerable, and performing Signs and wonders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Muratorian_fragment" title="Muratorian fragment">Muratorian fragment</a> mentions "the departure of Paul from the city [of Rome] [5a] (39) when he journeyed to Spain".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visits_to_Jerusalem_in_Acts_and_the_epistles">Visits to Jerusalem in Acts and the epistles</span></h4>

</p>
