<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_visit_to_Jerusalem_and_arrest">Last visit to Jerusalem and arrest</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_arrested.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1938" data-file-width="1598" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Paul_arrested.jpg/220px-Paul_arrested.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Paul_arrested.jpg/330px-Paul_arrested.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Paul_arrested.jpg/440px-Paul_arrested.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_arrested.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saint Paul arrested, early 1900s Bible illustration</div></div></div>
<p>In 57 AD, upon completion of his third missionary journey, Paul arrived in Jerusalem for his fifth and final visit with a collection of money for the local community. The Acts of the Apostles reports that he initially was warmly received. However, Acts goes on to recount how Paul was warned by <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">James</a> and the elders that he was gaining a reputation for being <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism" title="Antinomianism">against the Law</a>, saying "they have been told about you that you teach all the Jews living among the Gentiles to forsake Moses, and that you tell them not to circumcise their children or observe the customs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup> Paul underwent a <a href="/wiki/Ritual_washing_in_Judaism" title="Ritual washing in Judaism">purification ritual</a> so that "all will know that there is nothing in what they have been told about you, but that you yourself observe and guard the law."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the seven days of the purification ritual were almost completed, some "Jews from Asia" (most likely from <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Roman Asia</a>) accused Paul of defiling the temple by bringing gentiles into it. He was seized and dragged out of the temple by an angry mob. When the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a> heard of the uproar, he and some <a href="/wiki/Centurion" title="Centurion">centurions</a> and soldiers rushed to the area. Unable to determine his identity and the cause of the uproar, they placed him in chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[171]</a></sup> He was about to be <a href="/wiki/Protective_custody" title="Protective custody">taken into the barracks</a> when he asked to speak to the people. He was given permission by the Romans and proceeded to tell his story. After a while, the crowd responded. "Up to this point they listened to him, but then they shouted, 'Away with such a fellow from the earth! For he should not be allowed to live.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[172]</a></sup> The tribune ordered that Paul be brought into the barracks and questioned by <a href="/wiki/Flagellation" title="Flagellation">flogging</a>. Paul asserted his <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">Roman citizenship</a>, which would <a href="/wiki/Valerian_and_Porcian_laws#Porcian_laws" title="Valerian and Porcian laws">prevent his flogging</a>. The tribune "wanted to find out what Paul was being accused of by the Jews, the next day he released him and ordered the chief priests and the entire council to meet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[173]</a></sup> Paul spoke before the council and caused a disagreement between the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sadducees" title="Sadducees">Sadducees</a>. When this threatened to turn violent, the tribune ordered his soldiers to take Paul by force and return him to the barracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>The next morning, forty Jews "bound themselves by an oath neither to eat nor drink until they had killed Paul",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[175]</a></sup> but the son of Paul's sister heard of the plot and notified Paul, who notified the tribune that the conspiracists were going to ambush him. The tribune ordered two centurions to "Get ready to leave by nine o'clock tonight for Caesarea with two hundred soldiers, seventy horsemen, and two hundred spearmen. Also provide mounts for Paul to ride, and take him safely to Felix the governor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul was taken to <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">Caesarea</a>, where the governor ordered that he be kept under guard in Herod's headquarters. "Five days later the high priest Ananias came down with some elders and an attorney, a certain Tertullus, and they reported their case against Paul to the governor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[177]</a></sup> Both Paul and the Jewish authorities gave a statement "But Felix, who was rather well informed about the Way, adjourned the hearing with the comment, "When Lysias the tribune comes down, I will decide your case."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antonius_Felix" title="Antonius Felix">Marcus Antonius Felix</a> then ordered the centurion to keep Paul in custody, but to "let him have some liberty and not to prevent any of his friends from taking care of his needs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[179]</a></sup> He was held there for two years by Felix, until a new governor, <a href="/wiki/Porcius_Festus" title="Porcius Festus">Porcius Festus</a>, was appointed. The "chief priests and the leaders of the Jews" requested that Festus return Paul to Jerusalem. After Festus had stayed in Jerusalem "not more than eight or ten days, he went down to Caesarea; the next day he took his seat on the tribunal and ordered Paul to be brought." When Festus suggested that he be sent back to Jerusalem for further trial, Paul exercised his right as a Roman citizen to "appeal unto Caesar".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> Finally, Paul and his companions sailed for Rome where Paul was to stand trial for his alleged crimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECapesReevesRichards2011203_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECapesReevesRichards2011203-190">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg/220px-St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg/330px-St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg/440px-St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St._Paul%E2%80%99s_Grotto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St. Paul's Grotto in Rabat, Malta</div></div></div>
<p>Acts recounts that on the way to Rome for his appeal as a Roman citizen to Caesar, Paul was shipwrecked on "Melita" (<a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[181]</a></sup> where the islanders showed him "unusual kindness" and where he was met by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Publius" title="Saint Publius">Publius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[182]</a></sup> From Malta, he travelled to Rome via <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reggio_Calabria" title="Reggio Calabria">Rhegium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pozzuoli" title="Pozzuoli">Puteoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Two_years_in_Rome">Two years in Rome</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="890" data-file-width="1099" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png/220px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png/330px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png/440px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_235.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Paul Arrives in Rome</i>, from <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Die_Bibel_in_Bildern" title="commons:Die Bibel in Bildern">Die Bibel in Bildern</a></i></span></div></div></div>
<p>Paul finally arrived in Rome around 60 AD, where he spent another two years under house arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECapesReevesRichards2011203_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECapesReevesRichards2011203-190">[180]</a></sup> The narrative of Acts ends with Paul preaching in Rome for two years from his rented home while awaiting trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> wrote in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_2nd_century#Irenaeus" title="Christianity in the 2nd century">2nd century</a> that Peter and Paul had been the founders of the church in Rome and had appointed <a href="/wiki/Pope_Linus" title="Pope Linus">Linus</a> as succeeding bishop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[185]</a></sup> However, Paul was not a bishop of Rome, nor did he bring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianity_to_Rome" title="Christianity to Rome">Christianity to Rome</a> since there were already Christians in Rome when he arrived there;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[186]</a></sup> Paul also wrote his letter to the church at Rome before he had visited Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[187]</a></sup> Paul only played a supporting part in the life of the church in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1195" data-file-width="1939" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg/220px-Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg/330px-Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg/440px-Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decapitaci%C3%B3n_de_San_Pablo_-_Simonet_-_1887.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Beheading of Saint Paul</i> by <a href="/wiki/Enrique_Simonet" title="Enrique Simonet">Enrique Simonet</a>, 1887</div></div></div>
<p>The date of Paul's death is believed to have occurred after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a> in July 64 AD, but before the last year of Nero's reign, in 68 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1997436_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1997436-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="Second Epistle to Timothy">Second Epistle to Timothy</a> states that Paul was arrested in <a href="/wiki/Troad" title="Troad">Troad</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[189]</a></sup> and brought back to Rome, where he was imprisoned and put on trial; the Epistle was traditionally ascribed to Paul, but today many scholars considered it to be pseudepigrapha, perhaps written by one of Paul's disciples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown198431–46_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown198431–46-200">[190]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_I" title="Pope Clement I">Pope Clement I</a> writes in his <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Clement" title="First Epistle of Clement">Epistle to the Corinthians</a> that after Paul "had borne his testimony before the rulers", he "departed from the world and went unto the holy place, having been found a notable pattern of patient endurance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a> writes in his <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Ignatius_to_the_Ephesians" title="Epistle of Ignatius to the Ephesians">Epistle to the Ephesians</a> that Paul was martyred, without giving any further information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> states that Paul was killed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neronian_persecution" title="Neronian persecution">Neronian Persecution</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[193]</a></sup> and, quoting from <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Corinth" title="Dionysius of Corinth">Dionysius of Corinth</a>, argues that <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> and Paul were martyred "at the same time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> writes that Paul was beheaded like <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[195]</a></sup> a detail also contained in <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lactantius_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lactantius-206">[196]</a></sup><i> <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[198]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sulpicius_Severus" title="Sulpicius Severus">Sulpicius Severus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[199]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (July 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A legend later developed that his martyrdom occurred at the Aquae Salviae, on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Laurentina" title="Via Laurentina">Via Laurentina</a>. According to this legend, after Paul was decapitated, his severed head rebounded three times, giving rise to a source of water each time that it touched the ground, which is how the place earned the name "<a href="/wiki/San_Paolo_alle_Tre_Fontane" title="San Paolo alle Tre Fontane">San Paolo alle Tre Fontane</a>" ("St Paul at the Three Fountains").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VaticanBenedict_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-VaticanBenedict-210">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_LeonardisMasi199921_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_LeonardisMasi199921-211">[201]</a></sup> The apocryphal <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a> also describe the martyrdom and the burial of Paul, but their narrative is highly fanciful and largely unhistorical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1924_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1924-212">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Remains">Remains</span></h2>
<p>According to the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Liber_Pontificalis" title="Liber Pontificalis">Liber Pontificalis</a></i></span>, Paul's body was buried outside the walls of Rome, at the second mile on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Ostiensis" title="Via Ostiensis">Via Ostiensis</a>, on the estate owned by a Christian woman named Lucina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[203]</a></sup> It was here, in the fourth century, that the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> built a first church. Then, between the fourth and fifth centuries, it was considerably enlarged by the Emperors <a href="/wiki/Valentinian_I" title="Valentinian I">Valentinian I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valentinian_II" title="Valentinian II">Valentinian II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a>. The present-day <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Paul_Outside_the_Walls" title="Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls">Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls</a> was built there in the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VaticanBenedict_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-VaticanBenedict-210">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Caius_(presbyter)" title="Caius (presbyter)">Caius</a> in his <i>Disputation Against Proclus</i> (198 AD) mentions this of the places in which the remains of the apostles Peter and Paul were deposited: "I can point out the trophies of the apostles. For if you are willing to go to the Vatican or to the Ostian Way, you will find the trophies of those who founded this Church".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclHist_II.25_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcclHist_II.25-214">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> in his <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/De_Viris_Illustribus_(Jerome)" title="De Viris Illustribus (Jerome)">De Viris Illustribus</a></i></span> (392 AD) writing on Paul's biography, mentions that "Paul was buried in the Ostian Way at Rome".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-215">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, an 8-foot (2.4 m)-long marble sarcophagus, inscribed with the words <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"PAULO APOSTOLO MART"</i></span> ("Paul apostle martyr") was discovered during excavations around the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Paul_Outside_the_Walls" title="Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls">Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Ostiensis" title="Via Ostiensis">Via Ostiensis</a>. Vatican archaeologists declared this to be the tomb of Paul the Apostle in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESilver201318_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESilver201318-216">[206]</a></sup> In June 2009, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Pope Benedict XVI</a> announced excavation results concerning the tomb. The sarcophagus was not opened but was examined by means of a probe, which revealed pieces of incense, purple and blue linen, and small bone fragments. The bone was radiocarbon-dated to the 1st or 2nd century. According to the Vatican, these findings support the conclusion that the tomb is Paul's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_tradition">Church tradition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1544" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="393" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg/220px-Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg/330px-Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg/440px-Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_orthodox_mural_of_Apostle_Paul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a> mural painting of Saint Paul</div></div></div>
<p>Various Christian writers have suggested more details about Paul's life.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Clement" title="1 Clement">1 Clement</a>, a letter written by the Roman bishop Clement of Rome around the year 90, reports this about Paul:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>By reason of jealousy and strife Paul by his example pointed out the prize of patient endurance. After that he had been seven times in bonds, had been driven into exile, had been stoned, had preached in the East and in the West, he won the noble renown which was the reward of his faith, having taught righteousness unto the whole world and having reached the farthest bounds of the West; and when he had borne his testimony before the rulers, so he departed from the world and went unto the holy place, having been found a notable pattern of patient endurance.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFLightfoot1890">Lightfoot 1890</a>, p. 274, The First Epistle of Clement to the Corinthians, 5:5–6</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Commenting on this passage, Raymond Brown writes that while it "does not explicitly say" that Paul was martyred in Rome, "such a martyrdom is the most reasonable interpretation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownMeier1983124_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownMeier1983124-219">[209]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a>, who wrote in the 4th century, states that Paul was beheaded in the reign of the Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclHist_II.25_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-EcclHist_II.25-214">[204]</a></sup> This event has been dated either to the year 64 AD, when Rome was devastated by a fire, or a few years later, to 67 AD. According to one tradition, the church of <a href="/wiki/San_Paolo_alle_Tre_Fontane" title="San Paolo alle Tre Fontane">San Paolo alle Tre Fontane</a> marks the place of Paul's execution. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Catholic_liturgy" title="Catholic liturgy">liturgical</a> <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Saints_Peter_and_Paul" title="Feast of Saints Peter and Paul">solemnity of Peter and Paul</a>, celebrated on 29 June, commemorates his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martyrdom" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a>, and reflects a tradition (preserved by Eusebius) that Peter and Paul were martyred at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclHist_II.25_214-2"><a href="#cite_note-EcclHist_II.25-214">[204]</a></sup> The Roman liturgical calendar for the following day now remembers all Christians martyred in these early persecutions; formerly, 30 June was the feast day for St. Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler186630_June:_St._Paul,_the_Apostle_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler186630_June:_St._Paul,_the_Apostle-220">[210]</a></sup> Persons or religious orders with a special affinity for St. Paul can still celebrate their patron on 30 June.
</p><p>The apocryphal <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul" title="Acts of Paul">Acts of Paul</a> and the apocryphal <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Peter" title="Acts of Peter">Acts of Peter</a> suggest that Paul survived Rome and traveled further west. Some think that Paul could have revisited Greece and Asia Minor after his trip to Spain, and might then have been arrested in Troas, and taken to Rome and executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disputed_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-disputed-63">[note 4]</a></sup> A tradition holds that Paul was interred with Saint Peter <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ad Catacumbas</i></span> by the <a href="/wiki/Appian_Way" title="Appian Way">via Appia</a> until moved to what is now the <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Saint_Paul_Outside_the_Walls" title="Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls">Basilica of Saint Paul Outside the Walls</a> in Rome. <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>, in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_ecclesiastica_gentis_Anglorum" title="Historia ecclesiastica gentis Anglorum">Ecclesiastical History</a></i>, writes that <a href="/wiki/Pope_Vitalian" title="Pope Vitalian">Pope Vitalian</a> in 665 gave Paul's relics (including a cross made from his prison chains) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crypt_of_Lucina" title="Crypt of Lucina">crypts of Lucina</a> to King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oswy_of_Northumbria" title="Oswy of Northumbria">Oswy of Northumbria</a>, northern Britain. The skull of Saint Paul is claimed to reside in the <a href="/wiki/Archbasilica_of_Saint_John_Lateran" title="Archbasilica of Saint John Lateran">Archbasilica of Saint John Lateran</a> since at least the ninth century, alongside the skull of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Saint Peter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Conversion_of_Saint_Paul" title="Feast of the Conversion of Saint Paul">Feast of the Conversion of Saint Paul</a> is celebrated on 25 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul is <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">remembered</a> (with Peter) in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Festival_(Anglicanism)" title="Festival (Anglicanism)">Festival</a> on 29 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[214]</a></sup> Paul is considered the patron saint of London.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg/220px-PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg/330px-PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PaulusTarsus_LKANRW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Facial composite of Saint Paul, created by experts of the <a href="/wiki/Landeskriminalamt" title="Landeskriminalamt">Landeskriminalamt</a> of North Rhine-Westphalia using historical sources</div></div></div>
<p>The New Testament offers little if any information about the physical appearance of Paul, but several descriptions can be found in <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">apocryphal</a> texts. In the Acts of Paul<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnstone1984447_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnstone1984447-225">[215]</a></sup> he is described as "A man of small stature, with a bald head and crooked legs, in a good state of body, with eyebrows meeting and nose somewhat hooked".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalherbe1986170_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalherbe1986170-226">[216]</a></sup> In the Latin version of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Paul_and_Thecla" title="Acts of Paul and Thecla">Acts of Paul and Thecla</a> it is added that he had a red, florid face.
</p><p>In <i>The History of the Contending of Saint Paul</i>, his countenance is described as "ruddy with the ruddiness of the skin of the pomegranate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBudge1901531The_History_of_the_Contending_of_Saint_Paul_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBudge1901531The_History_of_the_Contending_of_Saint_Paul-227">[217]</a></sup> The Acts of Saint Peter confirms that Paul had a bald and shining head, with red hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBudge1901501The_Acts_of_Saint_Peter_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBudge1901501The_Acts_of_Saint_Peter-228">[218]</a></sup>
As summarised by Barnes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes1844212_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes1844212-229">[219]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysostom" title="Chrysostom">Chrysostom</a> records that Paul's stature was low, his body crooked and his head bald. <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">Lucian</a>, in his <a href="/wiki/Philopatris" title="Philopatris">Philopatris</a>, describes Paul as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"corpore erat parvo, contracto, incurvo, tricubitali"</i></span> ("he was small, contracted, crooked, of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubits" title="Cubits">cubits</a>, or four feet six").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_Kallistos_Xanthopoulos" title="Nikephoros Kallistos Xanthopoulos">Nicephorus</a> claims that Paul was a little man, crooked, and almost bent like a bow, with a pale countenance, long and wrinkled, and a bald head. <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Chrysostom" title="Pseudo-Chrysostom">Pseudo-Chrysostom</a> echoes Lucian's height of Paul, referring to him as "the man of three cubits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism portal</a></li><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Of the 27 books in the New Testament, 14 have been attributed to Paul; seven of these are widely considered authentic and Paul's own, while the authorship of the other seven is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAune20109_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAune20109-230">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnRogerson20031274_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnRogerson20031274-231">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerkins19884–7_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerkins19884–7-232">[222]</a></sup> The undisputed letters are considered the most important sources since they contain what is widely agreed to be Paul's own statements about his life and thoughts. Theologian Mark Powell writes that Paul directed these seven letters to specific occasions at particular churches. As an example, if the Corinthian church had not experienced problems concerning its celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Agape_feast" title="Agape feast">Lord's Supper</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[223]</a></sup> today it would not be known that Paul even believed in that observance or had any opinions about it one way or the other. Powell comments that there may be other matters in the early church that have since gone unnoticed simply because no crises arose that prompted Paul to comment on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009234_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009234-234">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Paul's writings, he provides the first written account of what it is to be a Christian and thus a description of Christian spirituality. His letters have been characterized as being the most influential books of the New Testament after the Gospels of Matthew and John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[note 9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Date">Date</span></h3>
<p>Paul's authentic letters are roughly dated to the years surrounding the mid-1st century. Placing Paul in this time period is done on the basis of his reported conflicts with other early contemporary figures in the Jesus movement including James and Peter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiley200221_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley200221-236">[225]</a></sup> the references to Paul and his letters by <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Rome" title="Clement of Rome">Clement of Rome</a> writing in the late 1st century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonaldson201053_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonaldson201053-237">[226]</a></sup> his reported issues in Damascus from 2 Corinthians 11:32 which he says took place while <a href="/wiki/Aretas_IV_Philopatris" title="Aretas IV Philopatris">King Aretas IV</a> was in power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonaldson201039_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonaldson201039-238">[227]</a></sup> a possible reference to <a href="/wiki/Erastus_of_Corinth" title="Erastus of Corinth">Erastus of Corinth</a> in Romans 16:23,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBitner2015268_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBitner2015268-239">[228]</a></sup> his reference to preaching in the province of <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a> (which dissolved in 80 AD),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAndria2012271_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndria2012271-240">[229]</a></sup> the lack of any references to the Gospels indicating a pre-war time period, the chronology in the Acts of the Apostles placing Paul in this time, and the dependence on Paul's letters by other 1st-century pseudo-Pauline epistles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2010170–71_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2010170–71-241">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authorship">Authorship</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Pauline_epistles" title="Authorship of the Pauline epistles">Authorship of the Pauline epistles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1766" data-file-width="2401" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Probably_Valentin_de_Boulogne_-_Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Paul Writing His Epistles</i>, painting attributed to <a href="/wiki/Valentin_de_Boulogne" title="Valentin de Boulogne">Valentin de Boulogne</a>, 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>Seven of the 13 letters that bear Paul's name – <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Romans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Corinthians" title="1 Corinthians">1 Corinthians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2_Corinthians" title="2 Corinthians">2 Corinthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Galatians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippians" title="Philippians">Philippians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Thessalonians" title="1 Thessalonians">1 Thessalonians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Philemon" title="Epistle to Philemon">Philemon</a> – are almost universally accepted as being entirely authentic (dictated by Paul himself).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAune20109_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAune20109-230">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunnRogerson20031274_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnRogerson20031274-231">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerkins19884–7_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerkins19884–7-232">[222]</a></sup> They are considered the best source of information on Paul's life and especially his thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Four of the letters (Ephesians, 1 and 2 Timothy and Titus) are widely considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudepigraphical" title="Pseudepigraphical">pseudepigraphical</a>, while the authorship of the other two is subject to debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAune20109_230-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAune20109-230">[220]</a></sup> Colossians and 2 Thessalonians are possibly "Deutero-Pauline" meaning they may have been written by Paul's followers after his death. Similarly, 1 Timothy, 2 Timothy, and Titus may be "Trito-Pauline" meaning they may have been written by members of the Pauline school a generation after his death. According to their theories, these disputed letters may have come from followers writing in Paul's name, often using material from his surviving letters. These scribes also may have had access to letters written by Paul that no longer survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The authenticity of Colossians has been questioned on the grounds that it contains an otherwise unparalleled description (among his writings) of Jesus as "the image of the invisible God", a Christology found elsewhere only in the Gospel of John.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonaldHarrington200058_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonaldHarrington200058-242">[231]</a></sup> However, the personal notes in the letter connect it to Philemon, unquestionably the work of Paul. Internal evidence shows close connection with Philippians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ephesians is a letter that is very similar to Colossians, but is almost entirely lacking in personal reminiscences. Its style is unique. It lacks the emphasis on the cross to be found in other Pauline writings, reference to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> is missing, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_views_of_marriage" title="Christian views of marriage">Christian marriage</a> is exalted in a way that contrasts with the reference in 1 Corinthians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[232]</a></sup> Finally, according to <a href="/wiki/Raymond_E._Brown" title="Raymond E. Brown">R. E. Brown</a>, it exalts the Church in a way suggestive of the second generation of Christians, "built upon the foundation of the apostles and prophets" now past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown198448_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown198448-244">[233]</a></sup>
</p><p>The defenders of its Pauline authorship argue that it was intended to be read by a number of different churches and that it marks the final stage of the development of Paul's thinking. It has been said, too, that the moral portion of the Epistle, consisting of the last two chapters, has the closest affinity with similar portions of other Epistles, while the whole admirably fits in with the known details of Paul's life, and throws considerable light upon them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAherne1908_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAherne1908-245">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Apostle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="219" decoding="async" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Paul_Apostle.jpg/170px-Paul_Apostle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Paul_Apostle.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Apostle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox" title="Russian Orthodox">Russian Orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of the Apostle Paul, 18th century (<a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration</a> Church, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kizhi" title="Kizhi">Kizhi</a> Monastery, <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">Karelia</a>, Russia)</div></div></div>
<p>Three main reasons have been advanced by those who question Paul's authorship of 1 Timothy, 2 Timothy, and Titus, also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_Epistles" title="Pastoral Epistles">Pastoral Epistles</a>:
</p>
<ol><li>They have found a difference in these letters' vocabulary, style, and <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">theology</a> from Paul's acknowledged writings. Defenders of the authenticity say that they were probably written in the name and with the authority of the Apostle by one of his companions, to whom he distinctly explained what had to be written, or to whom he gave a written summary of the points to be developed, and that when the letters were finished, Paul read them through, approved them, and signed them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAherne1908_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAherne1908-245">[234]</a></sup></li>
<li>There is a difficulty in fitting them into Paul's biography as it is known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrett19634ff_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrett19634ff-246">[235]</a></sup> They, like Colossians and Ephesians, were written from prison but suppose Paul's release and travel thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>2 Thessalonians, like Colossians, is questioned on stylistic grounds with, among other peculiarities, a dependence on 1 Thessalonians—yet a distinctiveness in language from the Pauline corpus. This, again, is explainable by the possibility that Paul requested one of his companions to write the letter for him under his dictation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrat1911_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrat1911-37">[32]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span></h3>
<p>Although approximately half of the Acts of the Apostles deals with Paul's life and works, Acts does not refer to Paul writing letters. Historians believe that the author of Acts did not have access to any of <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Paul's letters</a>. One piece of evidence suggesting this is that Acts never directly quotes from the Pauline epistles. Discrepancies between the Pauline epistles and Acts would further support the conclusion that the author of Acts did not have access to those epistles when composing Acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman200698_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman200698-247">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams195722,_240_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams195722,_240-248">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>British Jewish scholar <a href="/wiki/Hyam_Maccoby" title="Hyam Maccoby">Hyam Maccoby</a> contended that Paul, as described in the Acts of the Apostles, is quite different from the view of Paul gleaned from his own writings. Some difficulties have been noted in the account of his life. Paul as described in the Acts of the Apostles is much more interested in factual history, less in theology; ideas such as justification by faith are absent as are references to the Spirit, according to Maccoby. He also pointed out that there are no references to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pauline_Epistles" title="Pauline Epistles">Pauline Epistles</a>, although Paul mentions him several times in the Acts of the Apostles.
</p><p>Others have objected that the language of the speeches is too Lukan in style to reflect anyone else's words. Moreover, George Shillington writes that the author of Acts most likely created the speeches accordingly and they bear his literary and theological marks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington200718_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington200718-249">[238]</a></sup> Conversely, Howard Marshall writes that the speeches were not entirely the inventions of the author and while they may not be accurate word-for-word, the author nevertheless records the general idea of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall198042_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall198042-250">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Christian_Baur" title="Ferdinand Christian Baur">F. C. Baur</a> (1792–1860), professor of theology at Tübingen in Germany, the first scholar to critique Acts and the Pauline Epistles, and founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C3%BCbingen_School" title="Tübingen School">Tübingen School</a> of theology, argued that Paul, as the "Apostle to the Gentiles", was in violent opposition to the original 12 Apostles. Baur considers the Acts of the Apostles were late and unreliable. This debate has continued ever since, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adolf_Deissmann" title="Adolf Deissmann">Adolf Deissmann</a> (1866–1937) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Reitzenstein" title="Richard Reitzenstein">Richard Reitzenstein</a> (1861–1931) emphasising Paul's Greek inheritance and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Schweitzer" title="Albert Schweitzer">Albert Schweitzer</a> stressing his dependence on Judaism.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views">Views</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint-Paul.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1880" data-file-width="771" decoding="async" height="415" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Saint-Paul.JPG/170px-Saint-Paul.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Saint-Paul.JPG/255px-Saint-Paul.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Saint-Paul.JPG/340px-Saint-Paul.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint-Paul.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Byzantine ivory relief, 6th – early 7th century (<a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_Cluny" title="Musée de Cluny">Musée de Cluny</a>)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-view">Self-view</span></h3>
<p>In the opening verses of Romans 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[240]</a></sup> Paul provides a litany of his own apostolic appointment to preach among the Gentiles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[241]</a></sup> and his post-conversion convictions about the risen Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup> Paul described himself as set apart for the gospel of God and called to be an apostle and a servant of Jesus Christ. Jesus had revealed himself to Paul, just as he had appeared to Peter, to James, and to the twelve disciples after his resurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[242]</a></sup> Paul experienced this as an unforeseen, sudden, startling change, due to all-powerful grace, not as the fruit of his reasoning or thoughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul also describes himself as afflicted with "a thorn in the flesh";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[244]</a></sup> the nature of this "thorn" is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are debates as to whether Paul understood himself as commissioned to take the gospel to the gentiles at the moment of his conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrell200630_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrell200630-257">[246]</a></sup> Before his conversion he believed his persecution of the church to be an indication of his zeal for his religion;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Philippians|3:6_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Philippians|3:6-258">[247]</a></sup> after his conversion he believed Jewish hostility toward the church was sinful opposition, that would incur God's wrath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009236_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009236-260">[249]</a></sup> Paul believed he was halted by Christ, when his fury was at its height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[250]</a></sup> It was "through zeal" that he persecuted the Church,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Philippians|3:6_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Philippians|3:6-258">[247]</a></sup> and he obtained mercy because he had "acted ignorantly in unbelief".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disputed_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-disputed-63">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Understanding_of_Jesus_Christ">Understanding of Jesus Christ</span></h3>
<p>Paul's writings emphasized the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucifixion</a>, Christ's <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parousia" title="Parousia">Parousia</a> or second coming of Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBromiley1979689_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBromiley1979689-80">[74]</a></sup> Paul saw Jesus as Lord (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">kyrios</i></span>), the true messiah and the Son of God, who was promised by God beforehand, through his prophets in the Holy Scriptures. While being a biological descendant from David ("according to the flesh"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[252]</a></sup> he was declared to be the Son of God by his resurrection from the dead.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/E._P._Sanders" title="E. P. Sanders">E. P. Sanders</a>, Paul "preached the death, resurrection, and lordship of Jesus Christ, and he proclaimed that faith in Jesus guarantees a share in his life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup>
In Paul's view, "Jesus' death was not a defeat but was for the believers' benefit,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup> a sacrifice which substitutes for the lives of others, and frees them from the bondage of sin. Believers <a href="/wiki/Participation_in_Christ" title="Participation in Christ">participate</a> in Christ's death and resurrection by their baptism. The resurrection of Jesus was of primary importance to Paul, bringing the promise of salvation to believers. Paul taught that, when Christ returned, those who had died believing in Christ as the saviour of mankind would be brought back to life, while those still alive would be "caught up in the clouds together with them to meet the Lord in the air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sanders concludes that Paul's writings reveal what he calls the essence of the Christian message: "(1) God sent his Son; (2) the Son was crucified and resurrected for the benefit of humanity; (3) the Son would soon return; and (4) those who belonged to the Son would live with him forever. Paul's gospel, like those of others, also included (5) the admonition to live by the highest moral standard: "May your spirit and soul and body be kept sound and blameless at the coming of our Lord Jesus Christ"."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Paul's writings, the public, corporate devotional patterns towards Jesus in the early Christian community are reflective of Paul's perspective on the divine status of Jesus in what scholars have termed a "binitarian" pattern of devotion. For Paul, Jesus receives prayer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[257]</a></sup> the presence of Jesus is confessionally invoked by believers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[260]</a></sup> people are baptized in Jesus' name,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[262]</a></sup> Jesus is the reference in Christian fellowship for a religious ritual meal (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Supper" title="Lord's Supper">Lord's Supper</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[263]</a></sup> in pagan cults, the reference for ritual meals is always to a deity), and Jesus is the source of continuing prophetic oracles to believers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHurtado2005134–52_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHurtado2005134–52-276">[265]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atonement">Atonement</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">Atonement in Christianity</a></div>
<p>Paul taught that Christians are redeemed from sin by Jesus' death and resurrection. His death was an expiation as well as a <a href="/wiki/Propitiation" title="Propitiation">propitiation</a>, and by Christ's blood peace is made between God and man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Atonement_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005Atonement-277">[266]</a></sup> By grace, through faith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[267]</a></sup> a Christian shares in Jesus' death and in his victory over death, gaining as a free gift a new, justified status of sonship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Krister_Stendahl" title="Krister Stendahl">Krister Stendahl</a>, the main concern of Paul's writings on Jesus' role, and salvation by faith, is not the individual conscience of human sinners, and their doubts about being chosen by God or not, but the problem of the inclusion of gentile (Greek) Torah observers into God's covenant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStendahl1963_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStendahl1963-280">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn1982n.49_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn1982n.49-281">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinlan20042_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinlan20042-282">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWesterholm20154–15_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWesterholm20154–15-283">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[note 10]</a></sup> "Dying for our sins" refers to the problem of gentile Torah-observers, who, despite their faithfulness, cannot fully observe commandments, including circumcision, and are therefore 'sinners', excluded from God's covenant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack199788–89,_92_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack199788–89,_92-286">[274]</a></sup> Jesus' death and resurrection solved this problem of the exclusion of the gentiles from God's covenant, as indicated by Romans 3:21–26.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack199791–92_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack199791–92-287">[275]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul's conversion fundamentally changed his basic beliefs regarding God's covenant and the inclusion of Gentiles into this covenant. Paul believed Jesus' death was a voluntary sacrifice, that reconciled sinners with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[276]</a></sup> The law only reveals the extent of people's enslavement to the power of sin—a power that must be broken by Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[277]</a></sup> Before his conversion Paul believed Gentiles were outside the covenant that God made with Israel; after his conversion, he believed Gentiles and Jews were united as the people of God in Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[278]</a></sup> Before his conversion he believed circumcision was the rite through which males became part of Israel, an exclusive community of God's chosen people;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[279]</a></sup> after his conversion he believed that neither circumcision nor uncircumcision means anything, but that the new creation is what counts in the sight of God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[280]</a></sup> and that this new creation is a work of Christ in the life of believers, making them part of the church, an inclusive community of Jews and Gentiles reconciled with God through faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[281]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/E._P._Sanders" title="E. P. Sanders">E. P. Sanders</a>, who initiated the <a href="/wiki/New_Perspective_on_Paul" title="New Perspective on Paul">New Perspective on Paul</a> with his 1977 publication <i>Paul and Palestinian Judaism</i>, Paul saw the faithful redeemed by participation in Jesus' death and rising. Though "Jesus' death substituted for that of others and thereby freed believers from sin and guilt," a metaphor derived from "ancient <a href="/wiki/Korban" title="Korban">sacrificial</a> theology,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sacrifice_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-sacrifice-296">[note 11]</a></sup> the essence of Paul's writing is not in the "legal terms" regarding the expiation of sin, but the act of "participation in Christ through <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">dying and rising with him</a>."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to Sanders, "those who are baptized into Christ are baptized into his death, and thus they escape the power of sin<span class="nowrap"> </span>[...] he died so that the believers may die with him and consequently live with him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup> By this participation in Christ's death and rising, "one receives forgiveness for past offences, is liberated from the powers of sin, and receives the Spirit."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_Judaism">Relationship with Judaism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle_and_Judaism" title="Paul the Apostle and Judaism">Paul the Apostle and Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Perspective_on_Paul" title="New Perspective on Paul">New Perspective on Paul</a></div>
<p>Some scholars see Paul as completely in line with 1st-century Judaism (a Pharisee and student of Gamaliel as <a href="/wiki/Historical_reliability_of_the_Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Historical reliability of the Acts of the Apostles">presented by Acts</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrr19152276_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrr19152276-297">[284]</a></sup> others see him as opposed to 1st-century Judaism (see <a href="/wiki/Marcionism" title="Marcionism">Marcionism</a>), while the majority see him as somewhere in between these two extremes, opposed to insistence on keeping the "Ritual Laws" (for example the <a href="/wiki/Circumcision_controversy_in_early_Christianity" title="Circumcision controversy in early Christianity">circumcision controversy in early Christianity</a>) as necessary for entrance into God's New Covenant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1977_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1977-298">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn1982_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn1982-299">[286]</a></sup> but in full agreement on "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_Law" title="Divine Law">Divine Law</a>". These views of Paul are paralleled by the views of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_law_in_Christianity" title="Biblical law in Christianity">Biblical law in Christianity</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; border-width: 1px; font-size: 90%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Paul redefined the people of Israel, those he calls the "true Israel" and the "true circumcision" as those who had faith in the heavenly Christ, thus excluding those he called "Israel after the flesh" from his new covenant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[288]</a></sup> He also held the view that the Torah given to Moses was valid "until Christ came," so that even Jews are no longer "under the Torah," nor obligated to follow the commandments or <a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">mitzvot</a> as given to Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="center-aligned" style=""><a href="#CITEREFTabor2013">Tabor 2013</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Paul is critical both theologically and empirically of claims of moral or lineal superiority<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[290]</a></sup> of Jews while conversely strongly sustaining the notion of a special place for the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Children of Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[291]</a></sup> Paul's theology of the gospel accelerated the separation of the messianic sect of Christians from Judaism, a development contrary to Paul's own intent. He wrote that faith in Christ was alone decisive in salvation for Jews and Gentiles alike, making the schism between the followers of Christ and mainstream Jews inevitable and permanent. He argued that Gentile converts did not need to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaize" title="Judaize">become Jews</a>, get circumcised, follow Jewish dietary restrictions, or otherwise observe Mosaic laws to be saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Paula_Fredriksen" title="Paula Fredriksen">Paula Fredriksen</a>, Paul's opposition to male circumcision for Gentiles is in line with Old Testament predictions that "in the last days the gentile nations would come to the God of Israel, as gentiles (e.g., Zechariah 8:20–23),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[292]</a></sup> not as proselytes to Israel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018-306">[293]</a></sup> For Paul, Gentile male circumcision was therefore an affront to God's intentions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018-306">[293]</a></sup> According to Hurtado, "Paul saw himself as what Munck called a salvation-historical figure in his own right," who was "personally and singularly deputized by God to bring about the predicted ingathering (the "fullness") of the nations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018_306-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hurtado.Fredriksen.2018-306">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Sanders, Paul insists that salvation is received by the grace of God; according to Sanders, this insistence is in line with Judaism of <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 200 BC</span> until 200 AD, which saw God's covenant with Israel as an act of grace of God. Observance of the Law is needed to maintain the covenant, but the covenant is not earned by observing the Law, but by the grace of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2014_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2014-308">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sanders' publications<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1977_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1977-298">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1983_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1983-309">[296]</a></sup> have since been taken up by Professor <a href="/wiki/James_Dunn_(theologian)" title="James Dunn (theologian)">James Dunn</a> who coined the phrase "The New Perspective on Paul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn198295–122_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn198295–122-310">[297]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N.T._Wright" title="N.T. Wright">N.T. Wright</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WrightNP_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-WrightNP-311">[298]</a></sup> the Anglican Bishop of Durham, notes a difference in emphasis between Galatians and Romans, the latter being much more positive about the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)#Mosaic_covenant" title="Covenant (biblical)">continuing covenant between God and his ancient people</a> than the former. Wright also contends that performing Christian works is not insignificant but rather proof of having attained the redemption of Jesus Christ by grace (free gift received by faith).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[299]</a></sup> He concludes that Paul distinguishes between performing Christian works which are signs of ethnic identity and others which are a sign of obedience to Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WrightNP_311-1"><a href="#cite_note-WrightNP-311">[298]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_to_come">World to come</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/World_to_come" title="World to come">World to come</a></div>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bart_Ehrman" title="Bart Ehrman">Bart Ehrman</a>, Paul believed that Jesus would return within his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2006_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2006-313">[300]</a></sup> Paul expected that Christians who had died in the meantime would be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" title="Resurrection of the Dead">resurrected</a> to share in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">God's kingdom</a>, and he believed that the saved would be transformed, assuming heavenly, imperishable bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul's teaching about the end of the world is expressed most clearly in his <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="First Epistle to the Thessalonians">first</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="Second Epistle to the Thessalonians">second</a> letters to the Christian community of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thessalonica" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>. He assures them that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">the dead will rise first</a> and be followed by those left alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[302]</a></sup> This suggests an imminent end but he is unspecific about times and seasons and encourages his hearers to expect a delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowland1985113_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowland1985113-316">[303]</a></sup> The form of the end will be a battle between Jesus and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_of_Sin" title="Man of Sin">man of lawlessness</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[304]</a></sup> whose conclusion is the triumph of Christ.
</p><p>Before his conversion he believed God's messiah would put an end to the old age of evil, and initiate a new age of righteousness; after his conversion, he believed this would happen in stages that had begun with the resurrection of Jesus, but the old age would continue until Jesus returns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell2009236_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell2009236-260">[249]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg/220px-Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg/330px-Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg/440px-Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attributed_to_Lucas_van_Leyden_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paul the Apostle, (16th-century) attributed to <a href="/wiki/Lucas_van_Leyden" title="Lucas van Leyden">Lucas van Leyden</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle_and_women" title="Paul the Apostle and women">Paul the Apostle and women</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Timothy_2:12_(%22I_suffer_not_a_woman%22)" title='1 Timothy 2:12 ("I suffer not a woman")'>1 Timothy 2:12 ("I suffer not a woman")</a></div>
<p>The second chapter of the first letter to Timothy—one of the six disputed letters—is used by many churches to deny women a vote in church affairs, reject women from serving as teachers of adult Bible classes, prevent them from serving as missionaries, and generally disenfranchise women from the duties and privileges of church leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKroegerKroeger1998_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKroegerKroeger1998-319">[306]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p><sup>9</sup>In like manner also, that women adorn themselves in modest apparel, with shamefacedness and sobriety; not with broided hair, or gold, or pearls, or costly array;<br/>
<sup>10</sup>But (which becometh women professing godliness) with good works.<br/>
<sup>11</sup>Let the woman learn in silence with all subjection.<br/>
<sup>12</sup>But I suffer not a woman to teach, nor to usurp authority over the man, but to be in silence.<br/>
<sup>13</sup>For Adam was first formed, then Eve.<br/>
<sup>14</sup>And Adam was not deceived, but the woman being deceived was in the transgression.<br/>
<sup>15</sup>Notwithstanding she shall be saved in childbearing, if they continue in faith and charity and holiness with sobriety.
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>1 Timothy 2:9–15<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[307]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
