<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_James_Bible" title="King James Bible">King James Bible</a> (Authorised Version) translation of this passage taken literally says that women in the churches are to have no leadership roles vis-à-vis men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20065–10_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20065–10-321">[308]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fuller_Theological_Seminary" title="Fuller Theological Seminary">Fuller Seminary</a> theologian J. R. Daniel Kirk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirk_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-322">[309]</a></sup> finds evidence in Paul's letters of a much more inclusive view of women. He writes that Romans 16 is a tremendously important witness to the important role of women in the early church. Paul praises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoebe_(Bible)" title="Phoebe (Bible)">Phoebe</a> for her work as a <a href="/wiki/Deaconess" title="Deaconess">deaconess</a> and <a href="/wiki/Junia_(New_Testament_person)" title="Junia (New Testament person)">Junia</a> who is described by Paul in Scripture as being respected among the Apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bibleref2|Romans|16:7_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bibleref2|Romans|16:7-61">[56]</a></sup> It is Kirk's observation that recent studies have led many scholars to conclude that the passage in 1 Corinthians 14 ordering women to "be silent" during worship<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[310]</a></sup> was a later addition, apparently by a different author, and not part of Paul's original letter to the Corinthians.
</p><p>Other scholars, such as Giancarlo Biguzzi, believe that Paul's restriction on women speaking in 1 Corinthians 14 is genuine to Paul but applies to a particular case where there were local problems of women, who were not allowed in that culture to become educated, asking questions or chatting during worship services. He does not believe it to be a general prohibition on any woman speaking in worship settings since in 1 Corinthians Paul affirms the right (responsibility) of women to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophesy#Christianity" title="Prophesy">prophesy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiguzzi200495–107_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiguzzi200495–107-325">[312]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Biblical prophecy is more than "fore-telling": two-thirds of its inscripturated form involves "forth-telling", that is, setting the truth, justice, mercy, and righteousness of God against the backdrop of every form of denial of the same. Thus, to speak prophetically was to speak boldly against every form of moral, ethical, political, economic, and religious disenfranchisement observed in a culture that was intent on building its own pyramid of values vis-a-vis God's established system of truth and ethics.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>Baker's Evangelical Dictionary of Biblical Theology</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-326">[313]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>There were women prophets in the highly patriarchal times throughout the Old Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-326">[313]</a></sup> The most common term for <i>prophet</i> in the Old Testament is <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">nabi</i></span> in the masculine form, and <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">nebiah</i></span> in the Hebrew feminine form, is used six times of women who performed the same task of receiving and proclaiming the message given by God. These women include Miriam, Aaron and Moses' sister,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[314]</a></sup> Deborah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[315]</a></sup> the prophet Isaiah's wife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[316]</a></sup> and Huldah, the one who interpreted the Book of the Law discovered in the temple during the days of Josiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[317]</a></sup> There were false prophetesses just as there were false prophets. The prophetess Noadiah was among those who tried to intimidate Nehemiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[318]</a></sup> Apparently they held equal rank in prophesying right along with Abraham, Isaac, Jacob, Moses, Elisha, Aaron, and Samuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_326-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-326">[313]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kirk's third example of a more inclusive view is Galatians 3:28:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is neither Jew nor Gentile, neither slave nor free, nor is there male and female, for you are all one in Christ Jesus.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Galatians 3:28<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[319]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In pronouncing an end within the church to the divisions which are common in the world around it, he concludes by highlighting the fact that "there were New Testament women who taught and had authority in the early churches, that this teaching and authority was sanctioned by Paul, and that Paul himself offers a theological paradigm within which overcoming the subjugation of women is an anticipated outcome".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirk2012_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirk2012-333">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>Classicist <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Stagg" title="Evelyn Stagg">Evelyn Stagg</a> and theologian <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stagg_(theologian)" title="Frank Stagg (theologian)">Frank Stagg</a> believe that Paul was attempting to "Christianize" the societal household or domestic codes that significantly oppressed women and empowered men as the head of the household. The Staggs present a serious study of what has been termed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Testament_domestic_code" title="New Testament domestic code">New Testament domestic code</a>, also known as the <i>Haustafel</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaggStagg1978_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaggStagg1978-334">[321]</a></sup> The two main passages that explain these "household duties" are Paul's letters to the Ephesians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[322]</a></sup> and to the Colossians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[323]</a></sup> An underlying Household Code is also reflected in four additional Pauline letters and 1 Peter: 1 Timothy 2:1ff, 8ff; 3:1ff, 8ff; 5:17ff; 6:1f; Titus 2:1–10<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[324]</a></sup> and 1 Peter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[325]</a></sup> Biblical scholars have typically treated the <i>Haustafel</i> in Ephesians as a resource in the debate over the role of women in ministry and in the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombis2005317–30_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombis2005317–30-339">[326]</a></sup>
Margaret MacDonald argues that the <i>Haustafel</i>, particularly as it appears in Ephesians, was aimed at "reducing the tension between community members and outsiders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2004109_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2004109-340">[327]</a></sup>
</p><p>E. P. Sanders has labeled Paul's remark in 1 Corinthians<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[328]</a></sup> about women not making any sound during worship as "Paul's intemperate outburst that women should be silent in the churches".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1977_298-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1977-298">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders1983_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders1983-309">[296]</a></sup> Women, in fact, played a very significant part in Paul's missionary endeavors:
</p>
<ul><li>He became a partner in ministry with the couple <a href="/wiki/Priscilla_and_Aquila" title="Priscilla and Aquila">Priscilla and Aquila</a> who are specifically named seven times in the New Testament—always by their couple name and never individually. Of the seven times they are named in the New Testament, Priscilla's name appears first in five of those instances, suggesting to some scholars that she was the head of the family unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAchtemeier1985882_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAchtemeier1985882-342">[329]</a></sup> They lived, worked, and traveled with the Apostle Paul, becoming his honored, much-loved friends and coworkers in Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeller2010_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeller2010-343">[330]</a></sup> In Romans 16:3–4,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[331]</a></sup> thought to have been written in 56 or 57, Paul sends his greetings to Priscilla and Aquila and proclaims that both of them "risked their necks" to save Paul's life.</li>
<li>Chloe was an important member of the church in Corinth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[332]</a></sup></li>
<li>Phoebe was a "deacon" and a "benefactor" of Paul and others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[333]</a></sup></li>
<li>Romans 16<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[334]</a></sup> names eight other women active in the Christian movement, including Junia ("prominent among the apostles"), Mary ("who has worked very hard among you"), and Julia</li>
<li>Women were frequently among the major supporters of the new Christian movement<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_homosexuality">Views on homosexuality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_in_the_New_Testament" title="Homosexuality in the New Testament">Homosexuality in the New Testament</a></div>
<p>Most Christian traditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vatican.va_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-vatican.va-348">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coptic.net_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-coptic.net-349">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carm.org_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-carm.org-350">[337]</a></sup> say Paul clearly portrays homosexuality as sinful in two specific locations: Romans 1:26–27,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[338]</a></sup> and 1 Corinthians 6:9-10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[339]</a></sup> Another passage, 1 Timothy 1:8–11, addresses the topic more obliquely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[340]</a></sup> Since the 19th century, however, most scholars have concluded that <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_Timothy" title="First Epistle to Timothy">1 Timothy</a> (along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2_Timothy" title="2 Timothy">2 Timothy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Titus" title="Epistle to Titus">Titus</a>) is not original to Paul, but rather an unknown Christian writing in Paul's name some time in the late-1st to mid-2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2000393_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2000393-354">[341]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins20024_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins20024-355">[342]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Pablo_(1606)_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg/200px-San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg/300px-San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg/400px-San_Pablo_%281606%29_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Pablo_(1606)_-_Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of St. Paul (1606) by <a href="/wiki/Gregorio_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Gregorio Fernández">Gregorio Fernández</a>. Paul is widely depicted in artwork.</div></div></div>
<p>Paul's influence on Christian thinking arguably has been more significant than any other New Testament author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanders2019_10-15"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanders2019-10">[8]</a></sup> Paul declared that "<a href="/wiki/Abrogation_of_Old_Covenant_laws" title="Abrogation of Old Covenant laws">Christ is the end of the law</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[343]</a></sup> exalted the Christian church as the body of Christ, and depicted the world outside the Church as under judgment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> Paul's writings include the earliest reference to the "Lord's Supper",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[344]</a></sup> a rite traditionally identified as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_communion" title="Christian communion">Christian communion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>. In the East, church fathers attributed the element of election in Romans 9<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[345]</a></sup> to divine foreknowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup> The themes of <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a> found in Western Christianity do not appear in Eastern theology.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauline_Christianity">Pauline Christianity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Pauline Christianity</a></div>
<p>Paul had a strong influence on early Christianity. Hurtado notes that Paul regarded his own <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christological views</a> and those of his predecessors and that of the Jerusalem Church as essentially similar. According to Hurtado, this "work[s] against the claims by some scholars that Pauline Christianity represents a sharp departure from the religiousness of Judean 'Jesus movements'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHurtado2005160_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHurtado2005160-359">[346]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marcion">Marcion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcion" title="Marcion">Marcion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcionites" title="Marcionites">Marcionites</a></div>
<p>Marcionism, regarded as heresy by contemporary mainstream Christianity, was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian" title="Early Christian">Early Christian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dualist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belief_system" title="Belief system">belief system</a> that originated in the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Marcion_of_Sinope" title="Marcion of Sinope">Marcion of Sinope</a> at Rome around the year 144.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[note 12]</a></sup> Marcion asserted that Paul was the only <a href="/wiki/Apostles_in_the_New_Testament" title="Apostles in the New Testament">apostle</a> who had rightly understood the new message of <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> as delivered by Christ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1995_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1995-361">[347]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marcion believed Jesus was the savior sent by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a>, and Paul the Apostle was his chief apostle, but he rejected the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">God of Israel</a>. Marcionists believed that the <a href="/wiki/Divine_retribution" title="Divine retribution">wrathful Hebrew God</a> was a separate and lower entity than the <a href="/wiki/Love_of_God" title="Love of God">all-forgiving God</a> of the New Testament.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Augustine">Augustine</span></h3>
<p>In his account of his conversion experience, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> gave his life to Christ after reading <a href="/wiki/Romans_13" title="Romans 13">Romans 13</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugustine2019354–430_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugustine2019354–430-363">[349]</a></sup> Augustine's foundational work on the gospel as a gift (grace), on morality as life in the Spirit, on predestination, and on original sin all derives from Paul, especially Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation">Reformation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a></div><p>In his account of his conversion <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> wrote about <a href="/wiki/Righteousness" title="Righteousness">righteousness</a> in <a href="/wiki/Romans_1" title="Romans 1">Romans 1</a> praising Romans as the perfect gospel, in which the Reformation was birthed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrmann2016475–488_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrmann2016475–488-364">[350]</a></sup> Martin Luther's interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Paul's writings</a> influenced Luther's doctrine of <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">sola fide</a></i>.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Calvin">John Calvin</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> said the Book of Romans opens to anyone an understanding of the whole Scripture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParker1993_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParker1993-365">[351]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_theology">Modern theology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; border-width: 1px; font-size: 90%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Visit any church service, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a>, and it is the apostle Paul and his ideas that are central – in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hymns" title="Hymns">hymns</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creeds" title="Creeds">creeds</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sermons" title="Sermons">sermons</a>, the invocation and <a href="/wiki/Benediction" title="Benediction">benediction</a>, and of course, the rituals of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Communion" title="Holy Communion">Holy Communion</a> or Mass. Whether birth, baptism, confirmation, marriage or death, it is predominantly Paul who is evoked to express meaning and significance.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="center-aligned" style="">Professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Tabor" title="James D. Tabor">James D. Tabor</a> for the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huffington_Post" title="Huffington Post">Huffington Post</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[352]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Pauline Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesuism" title="Jesuism">Jesuism</a></div>
<p>In his commentary <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Epistle_to_the_Romans_(Barth)" title="The Epistle to the Romans (Barth)">The Epistle to the Romans</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Der Römerbrief</i>; particularly in the thoroughly re-written second edition of 1922), <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barth" title="Karl Barth">Karl Barth</a> argued that the God who is revealed in the cross of Jesus challenges and overthrows any attempt to ally God with human cultures, achievements, or possessions.
</p><p>In addition to the many questions about the true origins of some of Paul's teachings posed by historical figures as noted above, some modern theologians also hold that the teachings of Paul differ markedly from those of Jesus as found in the Gospels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaccoby199814_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaccoby199814-367">[353]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barrie_Wilson" title="Barrie Wilson">Barrie Wilson</a> states that Paul differs from Jesus in terms of the origin of his message, his teachings and his practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2011chapters_9,_10,_12_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2011chapters_9,_10,_12-368">[354]</a></sup> Some have even gone so far as to claim that, due to these apparent differences in teachings, that Paul was actually no less than the "second founder" of Christianity (Jesus being its first).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDwyer199827_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDwyer199827-369">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWrede1907179_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWrede1907179-370">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>As in the Eastern tradition in general, Western humanists interpret the reference to election in Romans 9 as reflecting divine foreknowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul_48-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005St_Paul-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_Paul">Views on Paul</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_views">Jewish views</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg/220px-Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg/330px-Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg/440px-Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Paul_with_a_Scroll_and_a_Sword.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of Paul holding a scroll (symbolising the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">Scriptures</a>) and the sword (symbolising his martyrdom)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle_and_Judaism" title="Paul the Apostle and Judaism">Paul the Apostle and Judaism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a></div>
<p>Jewish interest in Paul is a recent phenomenon. Before the <a href="/wiki/Judaism%27s_view_of_Jesus#Positive_historical_reevaluations" title="Judaism's view of Jesus">positive historical reevaluations of Jesus</a> by some Jewish thinkers in the 18th and 19th centuries, he had hardly featured in the popular Jewish imagination and little had been written about him by the religious leaders and scholars. Arguably, he is absent from the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> and rabbinical literature, although he makes an appearance in some variants of the medieval polemic <a href="/wiki/Toledot_Yeshu" title="Toledot Yeshu">Toledot Yeshu</a> (as a particularly effective spy for the rabbis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[357]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, with Jesus no longer regarded as the paradigm of gentile Christianity, Paul's position became more important in Jewish historical reconstructions of their religion's relationship with Christianity. He has featured as the key to building barriers (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graetz" title="Heinrich Graetz">Heinrich Graetz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Buber" title="Martin Buber">Martin Buber</a>) or bridges (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Mayer_Wise" title="Isaac Mayer Wise">Isaac Mayer Wise</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claude_G._Montefiore" title="Claude G. Montefiore">Claude G. Montefiore</a>) in interfaith relations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[358]</a></sup> as part of an intra-Jewish debate about what constitutes Jewish authenticity (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Klausner" title="Joseph Klausner">Joseph Klausner</a> and Hans Joachim Schoeps),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[359]</a></sup> and on occasion as a dialogical partner (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Richard_L._Rubenstein" title="Richard L. Rubenstein">Richard L. Rubenstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Boyarin" title="Daniel Boyarin">Daniel Boyarin</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[360]</a></sup>
</p><p>He features in an oratorio (by <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Felix Mendelssohn</a>), a painting (by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Meidner" title="Ludwig Meidner">Ludwig Meidner</a>) and a play (by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Werfel" title="Franz Werfel">Franz Werfel</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[361]</a></sup> and there have been several novels about Paul (by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shalom_Asch" title="Shalom Asch">Shalom Asch</a> and Samuel Sandmel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[362]</a></sup> Jewish philosophers (including <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leo_Shestov" title="Leo Shestov">Leo Shestov</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Taubes" title="Jacob Taubes">Jacob Taubes</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[363]</a></sup> and Jewish psychoanalysts (including <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanns_Sachs" title="Hanns Sachs">Hanns Sachs</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[364]</a></sup> have engaged with the apostle as one of the most influential figures in Western thought. Scholarly surveys of Jewish interest in Paul include those by <a href="#CITEREFHagner1980">Hagner 1980</a>, pp. 143–65, <a href="#CITEREFMeissner1996">Meissner 1996</a>, <a href="#CITEREFLangton2010">Langton 2010</a>, <a href="#CITEREFLangton2011a">Langton 2011a</a>, pp. 55–72 and <a href="#CITEREFLangton2011b">Langton 2011b</a>, pp. 585–87.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</span></h3>
<p>In the 2nd (and possibly late 1st) century, <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a> was a competing religious tradition to Christianity which shared some elements of theology.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Elaine_Pagels" title="Elaine Pagels">Elaine Pagels</a> concentrated on how the Gnostics interpreted Paul's letters and how evidence from gnostic sources may challenge the assumption that Paul wrote his letters to combat "gnostic opponents" and to repudiate their statement that they possess secret wisdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagels1992_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagels1992-379">[365]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muslim_views">Muslim views</span></h3>
<p>Muslims have long believed that Paul purposefully corrupted the <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">original</a> revealed teachings of Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHindsonCaner2008280_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHindsonCaner2008280-380">[366]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Young200460_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Young200460-381">[367]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell2001235_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell2001235-382">[368]</a></sup> through the introduction of such elements as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">paganism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999276_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999276-383">[369]</a></sup> the making of Christianity into a theology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">the cross</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999255_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999255-384">[370]</a></sup> and introducing <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a> and the need for <a href="/wiki/Redemption_(theology)#Christianity" title="Redemption (theology)">redemption</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Young200464_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Young200464-385">[371]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sayf_ibn_Umar" title="Sayf ibn Umar">Sayf ibn Umar</a> claimed that certain rabbis persuaded Paul to deliberately misguide early Christians by introducing what Ibn Hazm viewed as objectionable doctrines into Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06-386">[372]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony201168_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony201168-387">[373]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> repeated Sayf's claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrann201065–66_388-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrann201065–66-388">[374]</a></sup> The Karaite scholar <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Qirqisani" title="Jacob Qirqisani">Jacob Qirqisani</a> also believed that Paul created Christianity by introducing the doctrine of Trinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06_386-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06-386">[372]</a></sup> Paul has been criticized by some modern Muslim thinkers. <a href="/wiki/Syed_Muhammad_Naquib_al-Attas" title="Syed Muhammad Naquib al-Attas">Syed Muhammad Naquib al-Attas</a> wrote that Paul misrepresented the message of Jesus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddell2001235_382-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddell2001235-382">[368]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Rida" title="Rashid Rida">Rashid Rida</a> accused Paul of introducing <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">shirk</a></i></span> (polytheism) into Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999276_383-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999276-383">[369]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ali_Jouhar" title="Mohammad Ali Jouhar">Mohammad Ali Jouhar</a> quoted <a href="/wiki/Adolf_von_Harnack" title="Adolf von Harnack">Adolf von Harnack</a>'s critical writings of Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999255_384-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaardenburg1999255-384">[370]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Muslim polemics, Paul plays the same role (of deliberately corrupting the early teachings of Jesus) as a later Jew, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Saba%27" title="Abdullah ibn Saba'">Abdullah ibn Saba'</a>, would play in seeking to destroy the message of Islam from within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony201168_387-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony201168-387">[373]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrann201065–66_388-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrann201065–66-388">[374]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPall201355_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPall201355-389">[375]</a></sup> Among those who supported this view were scholars <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> (who believed while Paul ultimately succeeded, Ibn Saba failed) and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> (who claimed that the Jews even admitted to Paul's sinister purpose).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06_386-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdang1996105–06-386">[372]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_views">Other views</span></h3>
<p>The critics of Paul the Apostle include US president <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deist" title="Deist">Deist</a>, who wrote that Paul was the "first corrupter of the doctrines of Jesus."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJefferson1854_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJefferson1854-390">[376]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_anarchism" title="Christian anarchism">Christian anarchists</a> <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammon_Hennacy" title="Ammon Hennacy">Ammon Hennacy</a> took a similar view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[377]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[378]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baha%27i_faith" title="Baha'i faith">Baha'i faith</a>, scholars have various viewpoints on Paul. Discussions in Bahá'í scholarship have focused on whether Paul changed the original message of Christ or delivered the true Gospel, with proponents of both positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[379]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew_language" title="Biblical Hebrew language">Biblical Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">שאול התרסי</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">Sha'ūl ha-Tarsī</i></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">بولس الطرسوسي</span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Σαῦλος Ταρσεύς</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Saũlos Tarseús</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Tarsuslu Pavlus</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Paulus Tarsensis</i></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-columns-2">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-persecution-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-persecution_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-persecution_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Acts 8:1 "at Jerusalem"; Acts 9:13 "at Jerusalem"; Acts 9:21 "in Jerusalem"; Acts 26:10 "in Jerusalem". In Galatians 1:13, Paul states that he "persecuted the church of God and tried to destroy it," but does not specify where he persecuted the church. In Galatians 1:22 he states that more than three years after his conversion he was "still unknown by sight to the churches of Judea that are in Christ," seemingly ruling out Jerusalem as the place he had persecuted Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalemartin_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-dalemartin-49">[44]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> knew the Letter to the Hebrews as being "under the name of Barnabas" (<i>De Pudicitia</i>, chapter 20 where Tertullian quotes Hebrews 6:4–8); Origen, in his now lost <i>Commentary on the Epistle to the Hebrews</i>, is reported by Eusebius<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclHist_VI.25_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcclHist_VI.25-24">[21]</a></sup> as having written "if any Church holds that this epistle is by Paul, let it be commended for this. For not without reason have the ancients handed it down as Paul's. But who wrote the epistle, in truth, God knows. The statement of some who have gone before us is that Clement, bishop of the Romans, wrote the epistle, and of others, that Luke, the author of the Gospel and the Acts, wrote it</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Paul's undisputed epistles are 1 Thessalonians, Galatians, 1 and 2 Corinthians, Romans, Philippians, and Philemon. The six letters believed by some to have been written by Paul are Ephesians, Colossians, 2 Thessalonians, 1 Timothy, 2 Timothy, and Titus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umc.org_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-umc.org-28">[24]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-disputed-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-disputed_63-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-disputed_63-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-disputed_63-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">1 Timothy, 2 Timothy, and Titus may be "Trito-Pauline", meaning they may have been written by members of the Pauline school a generation after his death.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The only indication as to who is leading is in the order of names. At first, the two are referred to as Barnabas and Paul, in that order. Later in the same chapter, the team is referred to as Paul and his companions.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This clause is not found in some major sources: <a href="/wiki/Codex_Sinaiticus" title="Codex Sinaiticus">Codex Sinaiticus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alexandrinus" title="Codex Alexandrinus">Codex Alexandrinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a> or Codex Laudianus</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Paul does not exactly say that this was his second visit. In Galatians, he lists three important meetings with Peter, and this was the second on his list. The third meeting took place in Antioch. He does not explicitly state that he did not visit Jerusalem in between this and his first visit.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-177">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note that Paul only writes that he is on his way to Jerusalem, or just planning the visit. There might or might not have been additional visits before or after this visit, if he ever got to Jerusalem.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-235"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-235">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSanders2019">Sanders 2019</a>: "Paul [...] only occasionally had the opportunity to revisit his churches. He tried to keep up his converts' spirit, answer their questions, and resolve their problems by letter and by sending one or more of his assistants (especially Timothy and Titus).

Paul's letters reveal a remarkable human being: dedicated, compassionate, emotional, sometimes harsh and angry, clever and quick-witted, supple in argumentation, and above all possessing a soaring, passionate commitment to God, Jesus Christ, and his own mission. Fortunately, after his death one of his followers collected some of the letters, edited them very slightly, and published them. They constitute one of history's most remarkable personal contributions to religious thought and practice.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-285"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-285">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDunn1982">Dunn 1982</a>, p. n.49 quotes <a href="#CITEREFStendahl1976">Stendahl 1976</a>, p. 2 "... a doctrine of faith was hammered out by Paul for the very specific and limited purpose of defending the rights of Gentile converts to be full and genuine heirs to the promise of God to Israel"

<a href="#CITEREFWesterholm2015">Westerholm 2015</a>, pp. 4–15: "For Paul, the question that 'justification by faith' was intended to answer was, 'On what terms can Gentiles gain entrance to the people of God?" Bent on denying any suggestion that Gentiles must become Jews and keep the Jewish law, he answered, 'By faith—and not by works of <i>the (Jewish) law</i>.'" Westerholm refers to: <a href="#CITEREFStendahl1963">Stendahl 1963</a>

Westerholm quotes Sanders: "Sanders noted that 'the salvation of the Gentiles is essential to Paul's preaching; and with it falls the law; for, as Paul says simply, Gentiles cannot live by the law'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[273]</a></sup> (496). On a similar note, Sanders suggested that the only Jewish 'boasting' to which Paul objected was that which exulted over the divine privileges granted to Israel and failed to acknowledge that God, in Christ, had opened the door of salvation to Gentiles."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-sacrifice-296"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-sacrifice_296-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Jewish Encyclopedia (1906), "The Mishnah says that sins are expiated (1) by sacrifice, (2) by repentance at death or on Yom Kippur, (3) in the case of the lighter transgressions of the positive or negative precepts, by repentance at any time<span class="nowrap"> </span>[...] The graver sins, according to Rabbi, are apostasy, heretical interpretation of the Torah, and non-circumcision (Yoma 86a). The atonement for sins between a man and his neighbor is an ample apology (Yoma 85b)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE.SIN_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-JE.SIN-294">[282]</a></sup>

The Jewish Encyclopedia further writes: "Most efficacious seemed to be the atoning power of suffering experienced by the righteous during the Exile. This is the idea underlying the description of the suffering servant of God in <abbr title="Isaiah">Isa.</abbr> liii. 4, 12, <abbr title="Hebrews">Hebr.</abbr><span class="nowrap"> </span>[...] of greater atoning power than all the Temple sacrifices was the suffering of the elect ones who were to be servants and witnesses of the Lord (<abbr title="Isaiah">Isa.</abbr> xlii. 1–4, xlix. 1–7, l. 6). This idea of the atoning power of the suffering and death of the righteous finds expression also in IV <abbr title="Maccabees">Macc.</abbr> vi. 27, xvii. 21–23; M. Ḳ. 28a; Pesiḳ. xxvii. 174b; <abbr title="Leviticus">Lev.</abbr> R. xx.; and formed the basis of Paul's doctrine of the atoning blood of Christ (<abbr title="Romans">Rom.</abbr> iii. 25)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE.ATONEMENT_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-JE.ATONEMENT-295">[283]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-360"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-360">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">115 years and 6 months from the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">Crucifixion</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>'s reckoning in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Adversus Marcionem</i></span>, xv</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-EcclHist_VI.25-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-EcclHist_VI.25_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs1" id="CITEREFEusebius1885">Eusebius (1885). <span class="cs1-ws-icon" title="s:Nicene and Post-Nicene Fathers: Series II/Volume I/Church History of Eusebius/Book VI/Chapter 25"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Nicene_and_Post-Nicene_Fathers:_Series_II/Volume_I/Church_History_of_Eusebius/Book_VI/Chapter_25">"Book VI/Chapter 25" </a></span>. <span class="cs1-ws-icon" title="s:Nicene and Post-Nicene Fathers: Series II/Volume I/Church History of Eusebius"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Nicene_and_Post-Nicene_Fathers:_Series_II/Volume_I/Church_History_of_Eusebius"><i>Church History</i> </a></span>. <li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Acts%2021:8–10&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">Acts 21:8–10</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Acts%2021:15&amp;version=nrsv" rel="nofollow">Acts 21:15</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.earlychristianwritings.com/text/1clement-lightfoot.html" rel="nofollow">1st Clement – Lightfoot translation</a> Early Christian Writings

1 Clem 5:5: "By reason of jealousy and strife Paul by his example pointed out the prize of patient endurance. After that he had been seven times in bonds, had been driven into exile, had been stoned, had preached in the East and in the West, he won the noble renown which was the reward of his faith, [5:6] having taught righteousness unto the whole world and having reached the farthest bounds of the West; and when he had borne his testimony before the rulers, so he departed from the world and went unto the holy place, having been found a notable pattern of patient endurance".

Where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lightfoot" title="Joseph Lightfoot">Lightfoot</a> has "had preached" above, the <a class="external text" href="http://www.earlychristianwritings.com/text/1clement-hoole.html" rel="nofollow">Hoole translation</a> has "having become a herald".

See also the endnote (3) by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Cleveland_Coxe" title="Arthur Cleveland Coxe">Arthur Cleveland Coxe</a> on the last page of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Ante-Nicene_Fathers/Volume_I/CLEMENT_OF_ROME/First_Epistle_to_the_Corinthians/Chapter_LIX." title="s:Ante-Nicene Fathers/Volume I/CLEMENT OF ROME/First Epistle to the Corinthians/Chapter LIX.">wikisource 1st Clement</a> regarding Paul's preaching in Britain.</span>
</li>

In this 1649 work by Eustache Le Sueur, the fiery apostle lifts his right hand as if scolding the audience, while clutching a book of scripture in his left. Among the rapt or fearful listeners are people busily throwing books into a fire. Look carefully, and you see geometric images on some of the pages.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.atitle=The+rise+and+fall+of+scientific+authority+%E2%80%93+and+how+to+bring+it+back&amp;rft.volume=567&amp;rft.issue=7748&amp;rft.pages=309-10&amp;rft.date=2019-03-18&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A81987842%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fd41586-019-00872-w&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2019Natur.567..309C&amp;rft.aulast=Crease&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+P.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1038%252Fd41586-019-00872-w&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APaul+the+Apostle"></span></li>
